THE TOP LINE
Bad things still lurk inside the world's big banks

By Brooke Masters, Companies Editor 
578 words
26 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
11
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Tidjane Thiam , Credit Suisse's relatively new chief executive, was really annoyed on Wednesday. Just five months after outlining a strategic plan to set the bank on a new course, he was having to try again - this time with deeper job cuts and bigger losses than previously expected.

Staff in the bank's markets division, he said, had hidden giant risky bets from senior management and the cost of unwinding those positions was a large part of the problem.

This behaviour was "unacceptable", Mr Thiam said. "There have been consequences. I don't want to name names, but there have been changes," he explained. "There is a new way to manage the bank, which we've been very explicit on."

Good on Mr Thiam for identifying culprits and trying to change the culture. But it is shocking that this kind of thing can still happen in 2016.

Poor tracking of banks' trading positions was a big part of the problem before the financial crisis - and fear of unknown exposures was a key reason the markets ground to a halt after the collapse of Lehman Brothers.

Ever since, bankers and regulators have sought to ensure every lender has up-to-date information about its holdings, particularly in its trading book. Analytics firms run around selling high-tech "dashboards" that are supposed to provide real-time monitoring of banks' various exposures.

So how could senior managers at Credit Suisse be unaware of its outsized, risky and hard to sell positions? Some of them dated back several years.

In fact, all sorts of unexpectedly bad things still lurk inside the world's big banks. JPMorgan Chase's $6.2bn London Whale trading loss in 2012 is one particularly famous example. But mistakes happen all the time. As many banks cut staff and try to rationalise computer systems, it is not unheard of for entire securities portfolios to fall through the cracks and be forgotten for many months.

Critics argue that slips such as these are evidence the banks are still too big to manage properly and should be broken up . In Israel, Moshe Kahlon, the finance minister, is championing a plan to force banks to sell off their credit card divisions.

Bank chiefs have hit back, arguing that some activities, particularly those that cross international borders, can be better handled by large institutions. They also complain of unrealistic expectations. "Can I know what every one of 257,000 people is doing? Clearly, I can't," Stuart Gulliver , the HSBC chief, told a UK parliamentary committee last year. He suggested he was being held to higher standards than the military or the church.

Misbehaviour happens everywhere, but in a well-run institution, managers spot problems early. Then measures are taken to limit the damage and make amends. Culture is key, but so are resources. After the Whale, JPMorgan spent an extra $1bn on controls.

In that context, Credit Suisse's other big announcement this week makes perfect sense. It has joined forces with Palantir, a CIA-backed artificial intelligence firm, to use data-driven behavioural analysis to root out rogue traders and insider dealing.

Mr Thiam says he wants to change Credit Suisse's culture. But if his employees don't feel comfortable admitting to problems, at least his bank will soon be a lot better at catching them.

brooke.masters@ft.com

MARKETS & INVESTING
AI tools prove a hard sell with fund managers; Analysis: Equities

By Robin Wigglesworth 
847 words
26 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

'Quantitative' financiers are sceptical that new technologies will help in battle for returns

Isaac Newton may have been one of the finest minds of all time, but he turned out to be a miserable investor. "I can calculate the motions of the heavenly bodies, but not the madness of people," he lamented after losing a fortune in the South Sea bubble .

Increasingly, however, technology-savvy investors think they can harness mathematics and computer science to predict financial markets. Some of the most advanced asset managers are turning to artificial intelligence , with investment algorithms that can autonomously learn, adapt and scour vast data sets for tradable patterns.

But some "quantitative" financiers, or quants , are sceptical that these tools are any more than a somewhat better mousetrap, and argue that areas such as "machine learning" are overhyped and AI used as a marketing gimmick.

"Everyone wants the Holy Grail, something they can invest in and it will make 1 per cent a month forever," says Ewan Kirk, head of Cantab Capital, a quantitative hedge fund. "I don't want to be cynical, but I am sceptical."

David Harding, head of Winton Capital, one of the biggest quantitative hedge funds in the world, is also doubtful that AI represents a quantum leap for the investment industry. "I'm not a Luddite, we're always interested in new ways to make money. But I have to be very sceptical because I constantly have world-class people showing me miracle cures that don't actually work," he says.

Improvements in computing power have revolutionised the investment world, with algorithmic traders and investors increasingly influential across markets. Money is pouring into computer-driven hedge funds that have consistently managed to parse signals amid market noise. As a result money managers are scrambling to hire computer scientists, often pitting them in direct competition for talent with Silicon Valley's tech giants and hot start-ups.

AI is at the forefront of this. The field has taken several leaps forward in recent years. Most notably, Google's DeepMind AI arm has created a programme that recently thrashed a legendary player of Go, an ancient Chinese game so complex that most experts had reckoned it would take at least a decade before a computer could beat a human champion .

The potentially wider applications of techniques used by the likes of DeepMind's AlphaGo algorithm have fuelled optimism that investment management could be on the cusp of another technological revolution, possibly similar in scale to the electronification of markets in the 1970s and 1980s.

"Machine learning and artificial intelligence is going to play a very large role in quant managers, but also with traditional asset managers that are aggressively expanding in this space," says Osman Ali, a fund manager at Goldman Sachs Asset Management.

Popular AI approaches such as machine learning can be used by computers to develop autonomously. For example, a machine learning algorithm can master a computer game such as Super Mario independently, at first playing the arcade classic randomly but quickly figuring out how the controls work.

There is therefore widespread enthusiasm for the potential of machine learning algos to find fleeting but profitable patterns in the vast sea of data.

"I think of algos as little children that can scale tremendously," says Brad Betts of BlackRock's Scientific Active Equity arm. "And you can teach them to read millions of books at the same time."

Yet scepticism, even among quants, is pervasive. They see areas such as machine learning and deep learning - the latter underpinned DeepMind's Go exploits - merely as extensions of techniques that have long been in use.

"Lots of people [have been using] techniques that could be called machine learning for decades," argues Robert Hillman, head of Neuron Capital. "There's a huge difference between image recognition and using AI in markets. Will this be a paradigm change for investing? I don't think so . . . It's not a fundamental change, it's an efficiency improvement."

Mr Kirk says that most common AI approaches are focused on pattern recognition, such as telling the difference between a cat and a dog in an image. But markets are dominated by noise and chaos; the patterns are harder to find.

"As a geek I'm super-excited about AlphaGo, but it's a big leap from beating a game with clearly defined rules and objectives [to] investing," he says.

Even quants that are cautiously optimistic on the future of AI in investing warn of pitfalls. Algorithms that may look ingenious and backrest superbly against historical data have a habit of unravelling when confronted with unforgivingly fickle financial markets.

"Playing Super Mario might not necessarily work for markets. If you hit the button you always know what will happen, but you don't in markets," says another quant at a large hedge fund. "It can take time for it to find the good trades and to optimise them. It can go through a lot of bad trades."

Markets
Proposal Aims to Curb Bank Risk Models; Basel Committee proposes limits on discretion institutions have to measure how much risk they can take

By Donna Borak in Washington and Margot Patrick in London 
755 words
24 March 2016
23:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Global regulators moved to further tighten the reins on banks, proposing limits on the discretion institutions have to measure how much risk they can take.

The compromise plan reached Thursday by the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision wouldn't allow banks to calculate their own credit risk from exposure to other banks, large corporations and stocks. The committee is counting on the nonbinding proposal to lessen potential risks to financial stability tied to the connections among global banks.

Financial institutions in Japan, Switzerland, Singapore and Scandinavia potentially have the most to lose from limits on using their own models to measure risk. The proposed rules aren't expected to have a big impact on the four largest U.S. financial institutions J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., Goldman Sachs Group Inc ., Bank of America Corp . and Citigroup Inc . U.S. regulators have already imposed tough new borrowing limits, and the Federal Reserve has implemented rigorous annual stress tests.

 The U.S. is in a better position to make this adjustment with these rules than the European and U.K. banks," said Mayra Rodriguez Valladares, managing principal at consulting firm MRV Associates LLC. But U.S. banks could face high hurdles if U.S. regulators stop them from using their own risk models altogether and instead use a common standard, a policy change Fed governor Daniel Tarullo has sought.

Related

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/fsb-tlac-buffer-for-systemically-important-banks-of-16-by-january-2019-1447057802" (Nov. 9, 2015)

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/basel-committee-to-stop-banks-gaming-risk-models-1446472711" (Nov. 2, 2015)

The Switzerland-based global rule-setting body stopped short of barring banks from using their own modeling. Under the proposal, banks would still be allowed to calculate capital held against corporate, mortgage, credit-card and retail exposures, a typically significant portion of a bank's assets.

The committee is separately considering imposing additional constraints by placing a cap in the range of 60% to 90% on all four asset classes to limit the benefit of banks being able to use their own models to weigh risk. A significantly higher floor could have a material impact on all banks.

The proposal kicks off a three-month comment period before any policy changes are finalized. After that, each country must begin their own process of applying the rules to their respective banks.

The move marks the latest big piece of work by the Basel Committee on regulatory changes that address problems exposed by the financial crisis. Further work by the 29-member body that sets standards for banks globally includes changes to operational and credit risk along with a leverage ratio that would require banks to fund all assets with a minimum amount of money.

Regulators, especially those in the U.S., consider it problematic to allow banks to measure their own credit risk and determine how much capital they need to guard against a possible default by a counterparty and the individual firm's exposure if there is a loss.

 Addressing the issue of excessive variability in risk-weighted assets is fundamental to restoring market confidence in risk-based capital ratios," said Stefan Ingves, chairman of the committee and governor of Sweden's central bank .

Policy makers argue that individual assessments often deliver varied results among institutions because the models are different. By applying a standardized approach, regulators will have the ability to set a stricter framework based on their own expectations of default and calculations of potential losses.

 It is a clear indication that Basel has renounced models other than for favored asset classes, like mortgages, where they want to give banks leeway to support social and economic policy goals," said Karen Shaw Petrou, a managing director of advisory firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

Brad Carr, deputy director for regulatory affairs at the industry group Institute of International Finance , said the issue needs close examination.

 For instance, there are corporates with strong balance sheets, for which there might be a better way to converge and improve modeling treatments than using a blunt standardized approach," Mr. Carr said.

The Basel Committee said the treatment of sovereign exposures, another hotly debated issue at the committee, would be handled under a separate review.

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Raet, a software and services provider for human resources processes and management, said HgCapital bought a majority investment from CVC Capital Partners and the company's management shareholders.

Raet, which was acquired by CVC in 2011, makes cloud-based software that can provide services such as talent searching, budget planning, payroll management and other administrative functions. The company also provides services for consulting, analytics and training.

Raet, of Amersfoot, Netherlands, aims to expand its portfolio of human resource offerings, as well as its presence internationally, according to a news release. The company currently has presences in the Netherlands, Belgium, Argentina, Peru, Spain, Chile and Colombia, according to the release.

Raet was advised by Rothschild, KPMG LLC and Stibbe, according to the release.

London firm CVC Capital invests at least $150 million from its European funds and at least $50 million from its Asian funds in industrial and service businesses.

HgCapital, of London, invests in four sectors: technology, media and telecommunications; services; industrials; and renewable energy.

Pro Private Markets
Norwest Venture Partners Invests in Medgate; Norwest Venture Partners has made a  large strategic investment  in Medgate Inc., a software-as-a-service provider of environmental, health, safety and risk management services.

By Laura Cooper 
426 words
24 March 2016
15:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Norwest Venture Partners has made a  large strategic investment" in Medgate Inc., a software-as-a-service provider of environmental, health, safety and risk management services.

Canadian growth-equity firm Georgian Partners and BMO Financial Group joined Norwest as co-investors in Medgate, according to a news release.

Toronto-based Medgate provides EHS software to companies in areas such as occupational health, safety, industrial hygiene, environmental management and ergonomics. Its clients include National Aeronautics and Space Administration, GlaxoSmithKline PLC, Deere & Co. and Suncor Energy Inc.

Robert Arditi, a Norwest partner, said the firm has been interested in the compliance industry, specifically in risk management.

 It's nicely profitable and growing at a healthy pace," said Mr. Arditi.  They didn't need to do a transaction--they chose to work with us because they saw the value we could bring."

Medgate provides software that aims to make workplaces healthier--including products that strive to reduce employee injuries and prevent deaths. Mr. Arditi said the product intends to increase productivity by fostering a healthier and safer employee base.

In terms of growth, Mr. Arditi said Norwest views Medgate as a consolidator in the fragmented EHS space. The company is expected to opportunistically consider acquisitions in the risk management space.

 What we see from speaking to customers is they are clamoring for ways to improve their internal productivity, prevent injuries and deaths," Mr. Arditi said.  We would look at any solutions that could help them do that."

Medgate said it will use the new capital to spur product expansion, further enhance its analytics capacity and to fund acquisitions to accelerate growth.

Previous to Norwest's investment, Medgate acquired U.K.-based Tempus Software Ltd.'s Cohort Occupation Health software business in 2012, according to a news release issued at the time.

Norwest makes both venture and growth equity investments in companies across the technology, consumer, health-care and services sectors, among others. The firm currently manages $6 billion in capital and has funded more than 575 companies since its inception, according to its website.

Earlier this year, the Palo Alto, Calif.-based firm raised Norwest Venture Partners XIII, a $1.2 billion fund. A news release by the firm at the time said the capital would be used to support its growing network of portfolio companies.

Goldman Executive Put on Leave

By Katy Burne and Justin Baer 
644 words
24 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. put its head of U.S. inflation trading on leave as it reviews the way certain trades were booked, according to people familiar with the matter.

Josh Schiffrin, who was named a partner in 2012, still is employed at the firm, the people said. He was placed on leave in January, some of the people said.

Mr. Schiffrin didn't immediately return calls seeking comment.

Goldman's compliance executives are seeking to determine whether Mr. Schiffrin's desk may have violated the firm's policies in booking certain inflation trades, in which government bonds and their derivatives are used as hedges, the people said.

The review is in motion, and it is unclear what the firm's conclusions will be, the people said. Mr. Schiffrin hasn't been seen on the trading floor in a few weeks, some of the people said.

A person familiar with the matter said the review is expected to conclude shortly and that Mr. Schiffrin will return to work.

Meanwhile, a trader who worked under Mr. Schiffrin, Igor Cashyn, has resigned, people familiar with the matter said. He declined to comment.

The incident comes as Goldman and other firms have faced a deep debt-trading slump, reflecting factors including a longer-than-expected period of low interest rates and broad volatility tied to concerns about the health of the global economy.

"It's been a very difficult environment" in bond trading, said Brian Kleinhanzl, an equity-research analyst covering banks at Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc., a unit of Stifel Financial Corp.

Fixed-income, currency and commodities revenue fell 9% in 2015 across the top 12 global investment banks including Goldman, according to analytics firm Coalition. The number of traders, salespeople and certain researchers on desks catering to those classes of trading -- known in Wall Street circles by their acronym FICC -- fell 4% last year.

Traders have fared no better in early 2016. The five biggest Wall Street banks, including Goldman, are expected to show a 16% decline in total first-quarter trading revenue, Credit Suisse Group AG analysts have said.

Even Goldman, whose commitment to debt trading hasn't wavered since the financial crisis, has been cutting jobs from its FICC department, paying smaller bonuses to those who remain and shrinking the balance sheet tied to its trading activities.

Earlier this year, the firm moved to trim as much as 10% of its fixed-income staff, a steeper cut than Goldman normally makes during annual cost-cutting.

Meanwhile, inflation-related market measures have come under scrutiny from traders and policy makers.

One widely watched measure, the five-year, five-year forward inflation expectation rate, fell last month to its lowest level since the financial crisis in the middle of the market panic over U.S. recession risk and banks' exposure to negative interest rates overseas.

In that episode, inflation expectations by some measures fell alongside U.S. stocks, oil prices and Treasury yields before swiftly recovering when the panic ended.

Minutes from recent Federal Reserve policy meetings spotlight central bankers' concerns about certain inflation measures remaining under their 2% longer-term objective. Inflation has been stuck below that goal for more than three years.

Big banks have beefed up their compliance departments in recent years in response to a flurry of new regulations on capital and risk-taking.

Goldman's total workforce climbed 8% to 36,800 in 2016 to its highest year-end total, in part because of new hires in its legal, compliance and technology groups.

"We're making a significant investment in regulatory compliance," Harvey Schwartz, Goldman's finance chief, said in a January conference call with analysts. "We think it's critically important. We actually think it's a competitive advantage."

Tech
Google  s Computing Service Lures High-Profile Clients; The division is trying to catch up with Amazon and Microsoft in the public cloud market

By Jay Greene 
759 words
23 March 2016
00:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Google 's business of selling time on its immense computer network is gaining high-profile customers.

The computing-on-demand division of Alphabet Inc . on Wednesday will announce that Walt Disney Co .'s Disney Consumer Products and Interactive Media is using the service. Sources say Alphabet will announce several more new customers at the Google Cloud Platform Global User Conference in San Francisco that starts on Wednesday.

In addition, an executive of Netflix Inc ., a Google cloud customer, is scheduled to speak at the event.

In the past month, Google Cloud Platform has "http:/www.wsj.com/articles/apple-using-googles-cloud-moving-some-data-from-amazon-1458239465": Apple Inc . "http://www.wsj.com/articles/google-cloud-lures-amazon-web-services-customer-spotify-1456270951".

The new customers represent important validation for Alphabet's computing-on-demand division when it is struggling to establish itself as a leading provider of so-called public cloud services, which offer metered computing power over the Internet.

Google Cloud Platform is working to catch up to rivals Amazon.com Inc .'s Amazon Web Services and Microsoft Corp .'s Azure cloud service, which hold greater market share. In particular, it is adding features intended to address the needs of large companies.

Google announced on Tuesday the addition of two new massive data centers, in Oregon and Tokyo, opening later this year. The company said those sites would be the first of more than 10 data centers to roll out through 2017.

Google 's relationships with Apple, Netflix , and Spotify are especially significant steps forward for the search giant's cloud effort. They are Amazon Web Services customers, and they continue to use that service as well.

Google Cloud Platform leader Diane Greene , who at the conference will make "http://www.wsj.com/articles/alphabets-google-taps-enterprise-it-veteran-to-run-cloud-business-1447976698", was hired in part to lure such customers. She previously cultivated similar clientele as she built VMware Inc ., a vendor of data-center software, into a onetime favorite of chief information officers.

Google declined to make Ms. Greene available for this article.

Amazon Web Services , which pioneered the cloud-computing business a decade ago, leads the public cloud market. Morgan Stanley estimates that the service generated $7.9 billion in sales last year, while Microsoft Azure, which has capitalized on longstanding relationships with corporate customers, posted $1.1 billion in sales. Google Cloud Platform tallied less than $500 million, according to Morgan Stanley .

 They are probably No. 3 in the market, but they are a weak No. 3," said Lydia Leong , an analyst with Gartner Inc .

Disney plans to use Google 's storage, database and application service to run  hundreds" of projects including Disney Life, a subscription service in the U.K., the company said.

Spotify said it opted for Google Cloud Platform because the service's data-analytics offerings may help it refine listening recommendations for its subscribers.

Nonetheless, analysts and industry observers believe Google hasn't sufficiently adapted its vast computing network, which is finely tuned to its own operations, for use by outsiders, Ms. Leong said.

 Its challenge is to figure out how to commercialize that platform so that it is consumable by customers," she said.

Moreover, Google trails Amazon Web Services and Microsoft Azure in wooing startups to develop ancillary services that can help customers manage their operations on the network or offer features such as additional security to make its cloud offering more complete.

Madrona Venture Group LLC , a Seattle-based venture-capital firm that invests in cloud startups, regularly gets approached by companies developing businesses that work with Amazon or Microsoft . But it never hears from companies building Google Cloud services, said Madrona managing director Matt McIlwain .

 There's not an ecosystem here [for Google Cloud Platform] in the way there is on AWS," Mr. McIlwain said.

Moreover, the service lags behind rivals in the basics of support such as monitoring and managing its cloud services, Gartner 's Ms. Leong said. Those meat-and-potatoes features are essential to winning corporate contracts, she said.

Ms. Greene will emphasize improvements in that area at the conference, according to a person familiar with the matter.

 If they are going to serve enterprises, they are going to need to have a culture change to meet enterprises where they are," said Ellie Fields, vice president of product marketing at Tableau Inc., which makes data visualization software for all three top cloud services.

Shalini Ramachandran contributed to this article.

FRONT PAGE - FIRST SECTION
C Suisse and CIA-backed data group in joint venture to foil rogue traders

By Kara Scannell in New York and Hannah Kuchler in San Francisco 
404 words
23 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
01
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Credit Suisse is joining forces with Palantir Technologies, the artificial intelligence company backed by the CIA's venture capital arm, to use data-driven behavioural analysis to root out rogue traders and insider dealing.

Banks have been anxious to improve compliance and surveillance after a string of trading scandals that shocked the industry in recent years - from a $2bn loss UBS suffered at the hands of a rogue trader to a  ¬4.9bn unauthorised trading loss at Socit Gnrale.

The joint venture with the Swiss bank is the first for Palantir , the Palo Alto-based group which counts In-Q-Tel, the CIA's venture capital arm, among its -earliest investors.

Credit Suisse and Palantir declined to discuss financial terms but said the new company, named Signac after the French painter, was an even partnership of cash, employees and intellectual property.

Palantir - whose products are already used by many large banks - and Credit Suisse - which developed an intelligence unit of former foreign exchange traders - are betting their partnership will stand apart from the field by analysing the behaviour of -individuals to better understand the big picture.

The venture will track traders' activities and try to identify aberrations both from their own norms and from those of their colleagues.

Credit Suisse said the UBS and SocGen trading scandals shared what they called "toxic combinations", including trading in internal accounts and a large number of "cancel and correct" trades. The new product will analyse those outliers and marry the data on them with behaviour analysis, such as cell phone usage and door key-card swipes.

"We decided that we have to monitor the behaviour of individuals," said Lara Warner, chief compliance and regulatory affairs officer at Credit Suisse. "When those 'common toxic combinations' come together at an individual level you get an early warning sign."

Credit Suisse is using the product to track its nearly 1,000 traders and plans to roll it out to cover its 4,000-plus relationship managers around the world. The joint venture, the partners hope, will in time be used by other banks.

Palantir is one of the highest valued private technology start-ups in Silicon Valley, after Uber and Airbnb, raising funds last year at a valuation of $20bn.

Bregal Sagemount Invests $40M in Open Lending; Growth-focused firm Bregal Sagemount has invested $40 million in Open Lending LLC to acquire a  significant  minority position in the automated lending services provider.

211 words
23 March 2016

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Growth-focused firm Bregal Sagemount has invested $40 million in Open Lending LLC to acquire a  significant" minority position in the automated lending services provider.

Founded in 2000 and based in Austin, Texas, Open Lending offers loan analytics, risk-based pricing, risk modeling and automated decision technology to automotive lenders in the U.S. through its flagship product, Lenders Protection.

New York firm Bregal in a statement said the funds will allow Open Lending to accelerate its expansion among financial institutions, credit unions and regional banks. Bregal executives Gene Yoon and Blair Greenberg will join the company's board. Financial terms of the transaction weren't disclosed.

Goodwin Procter LLP served as legal adviser to Bregal. Vaquero Capital LLC and Andrews Kurth LLP provided financial and legal counsel to Open Lending.

Bregal, with $650 million in committed capital, invests $8 million to $150 million per transaction into targeted sectors including software, digital infrastructure, health-care IT and services, business and consumer services, and financial technology and specialty finance.

The Property Report
U.S. Commercial-Property Sales Plunge

By Peter Grant 
753 words
23 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

U.S. commercial-real-estate sales plummeted in February, sending the clearest signal yet that a six-year bull market might be coming to an end.

Just $25.1 billion worth of office buildings, stores, apartment complexes and other commercial property changed hands last month, compared with $47.3 billion a year earlier, according to deal tracker Real Capital Analytics Inc. In January, sales were $46.2 billion.

Prices, which had been on a steady march higher since 2009, are beginning to plateau, and have started falling in certain sectors and geographies, according to analysts and market participants. An index of hotel values compiled by real-estate tracker Green Street Advisors, for example, was 10% lower in February than a year earlier, due in part to reduced business and international travel.

Overall, commercial-property values are leveling off. Green Street's broad valuation index was 8.7% higher in February from a year earlier, but in the previous year the index rose 11%.

"Clearly there has been a plateauing," said Jonathan Gray, global head of real estate for Blackstone Group, the world's largest private-property owner.

The question is whether February was a temporary blip or the beginning of a more-lasting pullback. The Green Street index, which tracks higher-quality property owned by real-estate investment trusts, is 24% above its 2007 peak and 102% higher than the trough it hit in 2009.

Mr. Gray and others emphasize that the commercial-property market is much healthier than before the 2008 crash. Rents, occupancies and other fundamental factors are improving for most property types, analysts say. New supply growth has been limited, they point out.

"It's too early to call the end of the cycle," Mr. Gray said.

Still, some are heading for the exit. For example, Radnor, Pa.-based Brandywine Realty Trust has sold $765 million worth of property this year, including Cira Square, the former U.S. Post Office Building in Philadelphia.

Gerard Sweeney, Brandywine's chief executive, said the real-estate investment trust is "accelerating" its property sales. "We've made the call that, given where we are in the real-estate cycle, now is a good time for us to be harvesting value by selling," he said.

The market has slowed primarily because of forces at work in the global capital markets rather than problems stemming from real estate itself. These forces, which also caused global stock markets to plummet in the first two months of this year, have made debt -- the lifeblood of real estate -- more expensive and more difficult to obtain.

The most striking sign has been the sharp decline in bonds backed by commercial mortgages. In 2015, about $100 billion of commercial mortgage-backed securities were issued. This year experts believe volume will fall to $60 billion to $75 billion.

Banks and insurance companies are filling part of the void. But they can charge more and be more selective, making loans primarily backed by trophy and fully leased buildings in strong markets.

"There are deals falling out of the system," said Josh Zegen, managing principal of Madison Realty Capital, an investment firm with more than $1 billion of loans outstanding. "I'm able to be very choosy."

The real-estate debt markets began to tighten at the end of last year as concerns grew about interest rates rising and new regulations on lenders, enacted in response to the world financial downturn, began to take effect.

Central banks eased up on their tightening of interest rates, but the real-estate debt market remained choppy at the beginning of the year as global stock and corporate-bond markets convulsed amid signs the Chinese economy was weakening.

As yields of junk bonds soared, real estate became a less attractive investment. At the same time, the spreads between real-estate borrowing rates and Treasury bonds widened greatly.

Today loans that would have been made with interest rates in the 4.5%-to-5% range now are being made above 5%. Borrowers who would have lent up to 75% of a property's value have reduced their so-called loan-to-value ratios to between 65% and 70%.

Those changes mean that many real-estate investments that would have made sense before no longer do.

"Buyers have been hearing 'no' from lenders for the first time in a while," said Jim Costello, senior vice president at Real Capital Analytics.

U.S. News: Some Home Loans Will Be Trimmed

By Joe Light 
778 words
23 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
A7
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Thousands of homeowners will be eligible to have their mortgage balances cut under a plan approved by the federal regulator of mortgage-finance companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, according to people familiar with the matter.

The plan approved by the Federal Housing Finance Agency marks the first time that Fannie and Freddie will reduce mortgage balances on a large scale for struggling homeowners since the housing crisis erupted. But it doesn't go as far as some housing advocates wanted.

Fewer than 50,000 "underwater" homeowners, who owe more than their homes are worth and are already behind in their mortgage payments, will likely be eligible, people familiar with the matter said.

Fannie and Freddie -- which don't make mortgages but rather buy them from lenders and wrap them into guaranteed securities -- would also forgive principal only in cases where they determine the companies would lose less money with that option than foreclosure or other foreclosure-prevention methods. In addition, the new program will likely be limited to mortgages whose outstanding principal balance is under a certain dollar amount, people familiar with the matter said.

Because of the plan's restrictions, it won't have a significant impact on the national housing market, said Moody's Analytics chief economist Mark Zandi.

Still "it makes economic sense to do for everyone involved. It will keep some stressed homeowners in their homes, reduce losses to Fannie, Freddie and thus taxpayers, and could if focused, buoy some hard pressed neighborhoods," Mr. Zandi said.

The plan could be announced within the next few weeks, the people said.

About 4.3 million borrowers were underwater as of the end of 2015, according to real-estate data firm CoreLogic, down from 12 million in 2009. Not all those loans are backed by Fannie and Freddie.

In the past, policy makers explicitly rejected reducing the principal of mortgages backed by Fannie and Freddie, which were bailed out during the 2008 financial crisis and put into a so-called conservatorship under the FHFA's direction. Conservatives and others have long worried that any program involving principal reduction would signal to homeowners that such steps might be used in future crises.

"Is the role of Fannie and Freddie to make sure your house doesn't decline in value? I don't remember seeing that in the charter," said Mark Calabria, director of financial regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute.

On the other hand, supporters of principal reduction say the tool is effective in getting some delinquent homeowners to start paying their mortgages again, which in the long-term can save taxpayers money. Some advocates say that the benefits can flow to the rest of the community, as the homeowner becomes more likely to spend to maintain their home.

"Homeowners behave differently when they feel like a glorified renter versus when they feel like they're an owner," said Julia Gordon, executive vice president of the National Community Stabilization Trust, a nonprofit that works in neighborhood revitalization.

Principal reduction at Fannie and Freddie has been debated for years. The Obama administration's signature foreclosure prevention program -- the Home Affordable Modification Program -- encouraged mortgage servicers to reduce principal in some instances.

But the FHFA is independent of the administration and has been slow to roll out a program. Edward DeMarco, FHFA director from September 2009 until January 2014, wouldn't use that option, citing the risk to taxpayers.

Mr. DeMarco, now a fellow at the Milken Institute, on Monday said the FHFA's research in 2012 indicated lowering a borrower's monthly payment mattered most to avoiding foreclosure and that other methods of modifying loans were more effective than principal reduction. Most underwater borrowers still paid their mortgages and "we didn't want to create an incentive for them not to be current," Mr. DeMarco said.

Some left-leaning housing advocates hoped that Mr. DeMarco's successor, former Democratic Congressman Melvin Watt, would move quickly on a broad principal-reduction program. Instead, Mr. Watt took a slower, more-measured approach, frustrating some advocates and lawmakers.

"It has been six years since Congress created FHFA, and in all that time, your agency has never, not once, permitted a family to reduce its principal," said Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) in a hearing with Mr. Watt in November 2014. Mr. Watt at the time said the agency would come up with a plan soon. Since then, the potential impact of a principal-forgiveness plan has greatly diminished, not just because of foreclosures but also because of rising home values.

Elliptic Enterprises Ltd ., a startup founded by three Ph.D.s in computer science, physics and mathematics, has raised $5 million in a Series A round for its bitcoin fraud monitoring service.

Paladin Capital Group , a Washington-based venture firm with a history of investing in security technology, led the round. Octopus Ventures reinvested in Elliptic.

As banks and others experiment with bitcoin, their compliance departments will try to avoid any connection with criminal uses of the digital currency.

 For distributed ledger technology to achieve widespread use, compliance departments and regulators will demand independent monitoring capabilities to combat insider trading, fraud, and money laundering," said Mariano Belinky, managing partner at Santander InnoVentures, in a statement.

London-based Elliptic, launched in 2013, says it uses graph analysis and machine learning to find illicit bitcoin transactions. The company operates in the U.S. and Europe.

The company maintains a database where it links anonymous bitcoin addresses with identifiable entities. Its customers include law enforcement agencies and financial institutions.

Chief Executive James Smith, who holds a computer science Ph.D. from Oxford University in natural language processing, started the company with Tom Robinson, a Ph.D. in atomic and laser physics from Oxford, and Adam Joyce, a Ph.D. in math from Imperial College of London .

Elliptic previously launched a secure storage service for bitcoin, similar to the services offered by Xapo Ltd.

UK COMPANIES
Santander rolls out voice-recognition via iPhone for customers on the move; Banks

By Emma Dunkley 
660 words
22 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Thousands of Santander bank customers will soon be able to use voice-recognition technology to tell their mobile phones to monitor and recount their spending habits and make payments on the move.

Santander claims to be the first lender in the UK to enable customers using an iPhone to talk to its banking app in a similar way to how people interact with Apple's virtual assistant, Siri.

In a "friendly" female voice, Santander's SmartBank app can tell customers how much they have spent going back up to a year, or within a certain timeframe, or at a particular shop.

Within the next six months, the bank plans to expand the functions available using voice technology to allow customers to make payments, set up account alerts or report lost cards.

The bank is planning for the app to function securely by analysing the account holder's voice and only responding to that.

Nathan Bostock , chief executive of Santander UK, said: "Technology is rapidly changing how customers bank and pay on the move."

A study by consultancy firm CACI showed that current-account customers visited their bank branch 427m times last year, less than half the 895m logins on a mobile app. Santander has reported a 48 per cent increase in the number of customers using its mobile app over the past two years.

The launch comes as other lenders race to create services based on artificial intelligence and voice biometrics, as more customers shun branches in favour of mobile banking.

This coincides with a shift towards "robo-advice" spurred by financial institutions coming under pressure to cut costs by reducing staff numbers.

HSBC and Barclays have recently revealed voice recognition, although this enables customers only to log on to accounts by speaking.

Royal Bank of Scotland has launched artificial intelligence, called "Luvo", to interact with bank staff and potentially serve customers in the future.

Other banks in the US and Europe, such as ING, have launched voicetechnology services as an option for customers to manage their accounts.

Sigga Sigurdardttir, head of customer and innovation at Santander, said the voice technology, provided by Nuance Communications, was programmed to evolve and become more intuitive the more it was used.

She said: "It's all about creating a seamless customer experience, in line with the trend of having more digital customers than before."

The app provides "simple service on the go" and "allows customers to be in control" of their account, she added.

Ms Sigurdardttir said the bank planned to expand the new app capabilities to android phone users soon.

Santander added it would continue to provide paper and online statements, although more people were expected to turn to mobile banking.

Video link

Lloyds launches mobile mortgage interviews

Customers of Lloyds Banking Group will soon have mortgage interviews in their home via video, as fewer customers visit branches and greater numbers opt instead for mobile access.

The state-backed bank is launching its mortgage advice service across a video link that is accessible through a laptop, desktop or tablet computer.

The video service, which was piloted last year, enables customers to have a face-to-face interview online, remov-ing the need to travel to a branch or speak to an adviser by phone. It comes as banks are scaling down networks to cut costs. Lloyds said last month it would close 29 branches as part of plans to shut 200 over three years.

Lloyds is not alone. Jes Staley , chief executive of competitor Barclays, said after announcing a net full-year loss for 2015 that his bank "has not reduced its real estate footprint since the crisis" and that there were "tremendous savings for us to do so".

Figures from consultants CACI show that current-account customers visited their bank branch 427m times last year, compared with 895m logins on a mobile app.

Pro Private Markets
Firms Set Sights on  Fourth Industrial Revolution ; The European industrial and manufacturing sector has traditionally conjured up images of Sheffield steel mills and German automotive factories churning out cars in Rhine-Ruhr.

By William Louch 
1,595 words
22 March 2016
13:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The European industrial and manufacturing sector has traditionally conjured up images of Sheffield steel mills and German automotive factories churning out cars in Rhine-Ruhr.

Private-equity investment in the sector has typically focused on heavy industry, for example veteran buyout firm Clayton Dubilier & Rice's $1.7 billion acquisition of German packaging manufacturer Mauser in 2014 and Bain Capital's acquisition of Leicestershire-based brickmaker Ibstock.

While the tech industry continues to boom years after the 2000 dot-com bubble burst, the industrial sector has remained relatively steady but shown few signs of growth in the past five to six years.

Deal flow is still down compared with pre-financial crisis highs. The number of deals done has stayed relatively flat at between 83 and 120 each year since 2010, compared with 142 deals in 2006 and 135 deals in 2007, according to data provider Dealogic.

However, there are signs that industry is changing. According to a June 2015 report by consultancy firm McKinsey & Co., the industrial and manufacturing sector is experiencing  the fourth major upheaval in modern manufacturing," following the lean or  streamlining" revolution of the 1970s, the outsourcing phenomenon of the 1990s, and the rise of automation in the 2000s. This shift is known as Industry 4.0, in which advances in data exchange and automation are transforming manufacturing processes.

Big data, improved analytics and increasing computer power and connectivity are all driving this shift in traditional manufacturing and production methods. Fredrik Henzler, partner and co-head of industry value creation at Partners Group, said:  The development will have a tremendous impact on the industrials and manufacturing sector as technology can be used to reduce both the cost of production and the time it takes to bring new products to market."

While new technologies are expected to boost industrial companies' output and make production more efficient, they are not the sweet spot for private-equity investment, according to Mr. Henzler.

New technology alone won't necessarily result in direct profit growth among downstream manufacturers. This doesn't mean that there are no gains to be made in traditional manufacturing. Indeed, Nick Sealy, co-head of European investment banking at Baird, said:  Other sectors may offer higher organic growth rates but high-quality industrial businesses can be more broad-based and deliver strong cash generation."

However, efficient production processes are likely to benefit all segments of industry, from companies focused on traditional heavy industry to those specialized in driverless cars, for example.

By contrast, upstream manufacturers of products such as sensors or data-sharing technology--which enables driverless cars to function--are likely to see a bigger upside. They represent a less mature market that therefore has greater potential for growth, according to Mr. Henzler. Private equity houses should concentrate their efforts on investing in such upstream companies that develop industry-changing technology, as they are most likely to profit from the move toward automation and the Internet of Things, he said.

In today's low-growth environment, companies in the industrial sector grow comparatively slowly relative to other sectors that are less closely tied to GDP, such as health care and tech. Low growth rates impact on national income, meaning consumers are less likely to have money to spend on expensive new products, such as cars.

 The industrials sector is more naturally coupled to GDP than many other sectors," said Andrew Cunningham, a managing director at DC Advisory.

Digital developments in the industrial and manufacturing sector are changing this, providing opportunities for industrial and manufacturing companies to decouple themselves from the general economic cycle and offer investors higher returns. If a company can  decouple" from the general economy then it will become more resilient and can deliver returns regardless of macroeconomic uncertainty.

The sensors sector--which according to Catalyst Corporate Finance was worth $85 billion globally as of autumn 2015 and has grown at a compound annual growth rate of more than 7.5% over the last three years--is a good example of this.

Self-driving cars are generating huge demand for sensors, which enable cars to accelerate, brake and steer with limited or no driver interaction. A report released by research service BI Intelligence last year predicted that there will be 10 million self-driving cars on the road by 2020--up from none at present. If demand for self-driving cars increases, so will demand for sensors. While there is an established market for cars, the sensor market has a larger opportunity for growth.

Private equity houses have been quick to spot the potential in this sector; snapping up a number of deals in the last couple of years. According to the recent sector update produced by Catalyst Corporate Finance, the majority of sensor deals have been valued at less than $200 million, showing the relative lack of maturity in the sector.

The largest deal in the sensor sector in the past two years was Carlyle and PAI Partners' acquisition of Custom Sensors & Technologies Group in a deal worth $900 million, which closed in April 2014. Other private equity-backed deals in the sector include Parkwalk Advisors acquisition of Cambridge CMOS Sensors in July 2015 and Endless's acquisition of troubled engineering firm Suir.

While there is plenty of opportunity for private-equity firms to capitalize on the changes under way in the industrial and manufacturing sector, they are not the only buyers looking to take advantage of them. The past year has seen strategic appetite for companies in the sector thrive: 70% of all mergers and acquisition deals involved trade buyers in 2015, according to a report produced by DC Advisory in spring 2016.

Tahseen Siddique, vice president of M&A research at Baird, said:  Strategic buyers are competing increasingly fiercely against private-equity firms for deals in the industrials sector and more often than not are winning processes as they can pay in cash and benefit from synergies."

Particularly notable is the increasing appetite from foreign buyers--with trade buyers from the U.S., China and Japan all looking to purchase companies in the industrial and manufacturing sector.

Mr. Cunningham said:  Another big trend we have seen relevant to private equity exits is the revival of interest in European industrial companies from U.S. trade buyers."

He cited the comparative strength of the dollar against the euro and the increased tax efficiencies generated when U.S. firms with cash trapped in Europe invest the proceeds in Europe as oppose to paying a dividend back to their parent companies in the U.S. According to DC Advisory's Industrials 2015 M&A review and 2016 outlook, U.S. purchasers led the way in terms of investment in European industrial companies for the second year in succession in 2015, acquiring 23% of businesses sold in the sector in Europe that year.

Fortune 100 company Honeywell, for example, bought Germany-based water and energy company Elster Group from British buyout firm Melrose last year for $5.1 billion, generating a 2.3 times return on equity.

 Another trend that you can see in many sectors, including in industrials, is that the Chinese buyers are getting very active," said Fredrik tting, a partner at buyout firm EQT's Munich office.

The recent downturn in China has similarly forced Chinese investors to look abroad for growth, with European companies a likely target. In the industrials sector particularly, Chinese companies target European, largely German firms with technical and engineering expertise that they can export back to their domestic market.

ChemChina's purchase of precision technology manufacturer Krauss Maffei in early January 2016, for example, provided it with access to core competencies in process engineering and automation.

Investors Go Bargain Hunting in the Oil and Gas Sectors

This year has so far been characterized by market turbulence, a slowdown in China and falling oil prices. Investments in the oil and gas sectors have been particularly hard hit, with earnings and cash flows on the decline.

Over-levered companies, potential bankruptcies and defaults are all possible in the energy sector in today's economic climate as companies run up against their covenants and need capital investment to keep afloat. EIG Global Energy Partners announced in March 2016 that it was to mark down the unrealized value in a 2007-vintage fund, acknowledging losses in value in its oil and gas assets.

For companies with exposure to the oil and gas sector this is disastrous, to the savvy investor it presents an opportunity. Andrew Cunningham, managing director at DC Advisory, said he expects an increase in distressed investing, with an emphasis on businesses exposed to oil and gas.

Falling valuations in the sector mean that oil and gas companies potentially offer better value than other sectors where entry multiples are higher.

Research from S&P Global Market Intelligence found that of all the sectors it covers in Europe, energy had the lowest implied enterprise value to Ebitda multiples. The sector average for energy stood at 6.8x for M&A activity in 2015, against a high of 17.5x in the financial sector, according to S&P data.

Mr. Cunningham added that while companies haven't yet seen a lot of activity in this area, it is something that is expected to heighten with greater frequency as the year develops.

Zodiac Raises $3M Seed for Predictive Customer Analytics ; Zodiac Inc. has raised $3 million in seed funding for its predictive marketing analytics  software designed to help marketers home in on the most valuable customers. First Round Capital led the round, with participation from Metamorphic Ventures, Felicis Ventures and Western Technology Investment.

By Patience Haggin 
418 words

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Zodiac Inc. has raised $3 million in seed funding for its predictive marketing analytics software designed to help marketers home in on the most valuable customers. First Round Capital led the round, with participation from Metamorphic Ventures, Felicis Ventures and Western Technology Investment.

Zodiac, previously called CLVMetrics, requires three pieces of information from the marketer: a customer ID, a time- stamp record of transactions and a record of transaction values, according to Chief Executive Artem Mariychin. Zodiac then uses probabilistic models to predict each customer's likelihood of transacting with the company in the future and about the  basket size" of each transaction. It uses these predictions to forecast a  customer lifetime value," based on a forward-looking prediction of that customer's future spending.

Marketers can then measure the effect of every marketing step they take--such as sending a targeted email--on the individual's customer lifetime value. Marketers might identify a segment of customers whose lifetime value has decreased in the past six months, and then run a targeted campaign to win them back.

Zodiac works with brick-and-mortar retailers as well as online ones. Mr. Mariychin said Zodiac can work with any retailer where the customer can be linked to one ID, such as a credit card or a rewards card. Zodiac operates on a cloud-based software-as-a-service business model, charging clients a monthly subscription fee based on their number of customers.

Mr. Mariychin founded the company along with marketing professor Peter Fader and two other data scientists from the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School of Finance. Zodiac says its software is currently in use by retailers such as American Apparel and ModCloth.

The one-year-old startup received an investment from First Round Capital's Dorm Room Fund before this seed round. The new funding includes $1.5 million in equity financing from First Round, Metamorphic and Felicis, which closed in December. The remaining $1.5 million was raised as venture debt from Western Technology Investment, and closed in March.

Tracy s Take: Making Confidential Bank Oversight Less Confidential; The Wall Street Journal s financial regulation newsletter for Monday, March 21, 2016.

Tracy's Take: Making Confidential Bank Oversight Less Confidential

Congressional Republicans Preparing Fintech Promotion Legislation

Supreme Court Considers Hearing Challenge to Loan Rate Limits

CFPB Wins Final Judgment in Long-Running Morgan Drexen Battle

China Markets: Beijing's Support for Margin Trading Is Reborn

Making Confidential Bank Oversight Less Confidential

NEW YORK Federal Reserve Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer is fond of observing that bank supervisors are like spies: When they do their job well, the public doesn't notice.

Bank supervisors are thinking a lot these days about how to change that. If advocates for more inspection disclosure get their way, the public will have a clearer window than it currently does into problems at large institutions and what supervisors are doing to address them though the very prospect makes bank executives squirm.

For supervisors, some of the desire for more disclosure is a product of the political climate. The public has a broad perception, persistent since the financial crisis, that bank oversight is weak. Hence, politicians like Democratic presidential candidate Bernie Sanders "http://www.nytimes.com/2015/12/23/opinion/bernie-sanders-to-rein-in-wall-street-fix-the-fed.html?_r=0"about how the Fed  has been hijacked by the very bankers it regulates."

William Dudley , president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, has been at "http://www.wsj.com/articles/dudley-denies-serious-problems-in-feds-regulation-1416588378". He hosted a conference Friday on supervision, part of a broader effort to convince the public of what Fed officials believe: Supervisors are doing a far better job keeping bank risk-taking in line than before the crisis, and they are continually striving to improve.

 We have come a long way since the financial crisis," he said at the outset of the conference, adding:  Reliance on confidential information and confidential actions can make supervision seem mysterious to outsiders, which complicates the evaluation of what policies may be effective or ineffective."

Mr. Dudley, who has been part of Fed efforts to increase transparency on monetary policy, didn't specifically advocate disclosing more about the supervision process. Privately, some supervisors are resistant. They think their closed-door critiques of banks can be effective, and worry that disclosure could politicize that work.

Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. Vice Chairman Thomas Hoenig, often a maverick, said at Friday's conference supervisors should disclose more about problems they find at banks. Doing so would catch banks' attention, he said, and  I think you [would] have an overall more responsive and more accountable banking system."

Andrew Bailey, chief executive of the Bank of England's supervision arm, the Prudential Regulation Authority , noted that regulators have already been moving in the direction of more disclosure. Decades ago, bank capital ratios weren't disclosed. Now, supervisors disclose those as well as a lot more, such as how banks fare under  stress tests" examining how their capital levels might hold up during a recession.

 We are publishing things on banks that our predecessors would have been horrified" to see in the public domain, he said.

Still, there are limits even to the most aggressive disclosure advocates. Mr. Hoenig doesn't want to disclose numerical grades of bank performance, which he said would turn supervisors into rating agencies. To be sure, banks already have to disclose material information to investors under securities laws.

The prospect of more public rebukes won't excite bankers. But it could give supervisors more credibility, which is ultimately in the interest of financial executives. Until populist anger about bank oversight lets up, supervisors' desire to prove they are getting tough won't either.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Congressional Republicans Preparing Fintech Promotion Legislation

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/congressional-republicans-preparing-fintech-promotion-legislation-1458333389?tesla=y"a legislative package aimed at helping financial-technology companies expand by shielding them from emerging regulatory constraints.

Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.) told reporters Thursday that Republicans are preparing a legislative package called  Innovation Initiative" that would outline a regulatory framework he said would encourage growth in  fintech" and online investor pools like crowdfunding and angel investing, who typically raise seed money for startups.

Supreme Court Considers Hearing Challenge to Loan Rate Limits

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/supreme-court-considers-hearing-challenge-to-loan-rate-limits-1458510846"as soon as this week whether it will accept a case that could prevent some lenders and debt collectors from overriding state laws that cap interest rates.

If the high court declines to hear the case, banks are braced for an intense legal battle over whether buyers of some loans can charge the same interest rates as the banks that issued them. The market is worth billions of dollars.

CFPB Wins Final Judgment in Long-Running Morgan Drexen Battle

Differentiating Regulation From Supervision

Setting out to model the difference between financial regulation and supervision, "https://www.newyorkfed.org/medialibrary/media/research/staff_reports/sr769.pdf?la=en" link the distinction between regulation and supervision to the difference between verifiability and observability. In its purest form, they say, regulation is written into law and enforced through courts, therefore requiring verifiability; in contrast, supervision has discretion and only requires observability. The model assumes that while a bank's possible actions aren't verifiable, some are observable by supervisors, meaning that supervisors can prevent some observable actions but have to rely on incentives to ensure the work is done properly. The model can speak to some issues of supervisory incentives, they say, and allows supervisory costs to include non-monetary costs like opportunity costs.

Basel Rules Fail to Account for Complexity

The Basel Committee 's standardized capital rules fail to take into account differences in national models, and the committee's habit of  papering over" those differences doesn't do anyone any good, "http://www.fedfin.com/blog/2058-karen-petrou-on-how-basel-blessed-russia-and-thus-damned-its-capital-framework" Ms. Petrou warns that by smoothing over national differences in banking systems Basel could jeopardize the efficacy of the rules intended to counteract systemic risk. She scoffs at the notion that national regulators would be able to fill out questionnaires essentially self-reporting as to whether they were in compliance with Basel guidelines, a policy that Ms. Petrou likens to a student grading her own test. The standardized capital rules Basel has laid out are far inferior to a system in which complexity plays more of a role, Ms. Petrou writes.

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/quest-for-stolen-bangladeshi-funds-leads-to-philippine-casinos-1458324994"warned the Philippines that there was a gaping hole in its defenses against people illegally moving money around its economy: the country's casinos. In recent days, financial investigators trying to track down and recover $101 million stolen in February from Bangladesh's account at the New York Fed in an elaborate cybertheft have been stymied by the fact that casinos aren't covered under the Philippines' Anti-Money Laundering Law.

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/indonesian-government-will-no-longer-bail-out-commercial-banks-1458542461"bail out commercial banks when they face financial problems, but will instead force the institutions to use their own assets or those belonging to shareholders to raise funds in a crisis, according to a new regulation Parliament passed late last week. If those assets are insufficient, the Indonesia Deposit Insurance Corporation, the country's official insurer of bank deposits, will loan money to troubled institutions using the premiums and other funds it collects from commercial lenders operating in the country.

Markets
IHS and Markit to Merge, Creating Data Heavyweight; Inversion deal to create new London-based firm called IHS Markit

By Shayndi Raice, Sarah Krouse and Anne Steele 
1,206 words
21 March 2016
20:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. information and analytics provider IHS Inc . and U.K.-based market-data company Markit Ltd . said they would combine to create a $13 billion company based in London.

By moving to the U.K., IHS will be able to take advantage of the country's lower corporate-tax rate through what is known as a tax inversion, a way for U.S. companies to avoid paying taxes at home. The new company, to be called IHS Markit, is expecting a corporate-tax rate in the low- to mid-20% range, compared with the 35% U.S. firms pay.

Monday's deal values Markit at about $5.8 billion, or $31.13 a share, a 5.6% premium to Friday's closing stock price.

After the deal is completed which is expected in the second half of the year shareholders of IHS , which has a market value of about $7.5 billion as of Friday's close, will own about 57% of the combined company.

Shareholders of London-based Markit will own about 43% of the firm. Although it will be located in London, the new company will have  certain key operations" in IHS 's base of Englewood, Colo.

Started in a barn more than a decade ago by former TD Securities credit-trading executive Lance Uggla, Markit aggregates information from major bond dealers that is used for research, valuation, trading and reporting about derivatives, bonds, loans and currencies.

The company has become an important provider of data to Wall Street and was initially backed by a dozen large lenders, including Bank of America Corp ., Goldman Sachs Group Inc ., Deutsche Bank AG and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co .

Related

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/why-this-financial-data-company-looks-to-have-undersold-itself-1458579564"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/markit-delivers-more-than-data-1447098905"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/markits-ipo-increased-in-size-priced-within-forecast-at-24-a-share-source-says-1403130660"

Jerre Stead , chief executive of IHS, will become CEO of the combined firm until his retirement on Dec. 31, 2017. He also will be chairman. Mr. Uggla, who will be president and a member of the board of directors, will take over as CEO after Mr. Stead's departure.

The two executives, who have been involved in more than 100 mergers and acquisitions over the past decade, discussed merging over breakfast in December after being connected by an investor in both companies, they said. By early January, the deal was progressing with a March 21 deadline to announce the merger.

Messrs. Stead and Uggla said the deal was attractive because their customer bases don't overlap and it represents an opportunity to marry corporate and financial data. IHS serves more corporate customers and Markit provides data to Wall Street banks and asset managers.

 We use content in slightly different ways," Mr. Uggla said.

IHS provides analytics for businesses and governments in more than 140 countries. Founded in 1959, it went public in 2005 and has about 9,000 employees in 32 countries. The firm has been on an acquisition spree in recent years, pursuing a growth strategy by snapping up data providers and rival analytics firms.

It completed four deals in 2015 and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ihs-acquires-opis-for-650-million-1452512380"Oil Price Information Service .

IHS sponsors one of the most important global energy conferences every year, IHS CERAWeek and retains some of the industry's top authorities, including Daniel Yergin , the Pulitzer-Prize winning author and economic researcher. Mr. Uggla said IHS 's energy data was particularly valuable to Wall Street investors trying to size up debt and equity investment opportunities.

IHS and Markit posted a combined $3.3 billion in revenue and $1.2 billion in adjusted earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization in fiscal 2015.

They also said they had identified cost savings of $125 million and revenue opportunities of $100 million by 2019.

What is IHS ?

IHS provides analytics for businesses and governments in more than 140 countries. Founded in 1959, it went public in 2005 and has about 9,000 employees in 32 countries. The firm has been on an acquisition spree in recent years, snapping up data providers and rival analytics firms. It divides its business into three segments:

* RESOURCES: IHS offers analysis on wells, pipeline miles, and regulatory and mineral rights documents, as well as data on oil and gas fields, basins, and power and industrial plants. Its information is used for capital expenditure, production, and capacity industry analysis and forecasts.

* TRANSPORTATION: Within the automotive sector, IHS offers data on original equipment manufacturers, parts suppliers and dealers. Specifically, it provides forecasts of sales and production for light vehicles, medium and heavy commercial vehicles, powertrain, components, and technology systems. As for maritime and trade, it provides data on nearly 200,000 ships, as well as monthly import and export statistics. Its aerospace, defense and security analytics offers specifications for military vehicles, naval vessels and aircraft types.

* MARKETS: IHS 's product-design services provide technical professionals with information to design products, complete engineering projects and address supply-chain challenges. Its offerings include analysis on engineering standards, codes, handbooks and other scientific and technical documents. IHS also offers data within its economics and country risk division to help professionals make decisions on capital investments, country-entry strategies, acquisitions, strategic planning processes, and production and sales forecasts.

Corporate tax inversions fell out of favor in 2014 after the U.S. Treasury Department instituted a series of rules that made it less profitable for companies to move overseas. The Obama administration portrayed companies leaving the U.S. as bad corporate citizens.

The government's move at that time managed to kill several deals, including a $50 billion-plus acquisition by U.S. pharmaceutical firm AbbVie Inc . of Irish drug company Shire PLC .

Still, tax-inversion deals have continued. More recently, the inversion issue has come up in the U.S. presidential election. Last week, presidential hopeful Bernie Sanders sent a letter to Treasury Secretary Jack Lew "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sanders-urges-treasury-to-set-tax-restrictions-on-inversion-deals-1458316802". He specifically called out Pfizer Inc .'s proposed merger with Allergan PLC . Candidate Hillary Clinton has criticized companies such as Johnson Controls Inc ., a Milwaukee-based auto-parts provider that is in the process of merging with Tyco International PLC that will see its legal domicile move to Ireland, which has a much lower corporate-tax rate.

One person involved in the deal said IHS and Markit don't expect the tax aspect of the deal to raise too many eyebrows in Washington.

The merger is almost one of equals, and the U.K. company's chief executive will lead the combined firm, ultimately, the person said. This suggests the deal is being pursued for strategic reasons and not simply to avoid paying U.S. taxes, the person said.

Anne Steele and Sarah Kent contributed to this article.
