Moat Raises $50 Million to Help Develop Digital Ad Currency; Analytics  firm aims to take on Google by creating a new currency for buying and selling online ads, similar to Nielsen for TV ratings

By Mike Shields 
929 words
21 March 2016
10:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Web media analytics company Moat has raised $50 million in new funding as it looks to take on an ambitious goal. It plans to challenge Google, Nielsen, comScore and other digital media companies by developing a standard metric that would serve as the currency for buying and selling digital ads.

Founded in 2010, "http://blogs.wsj.com/cmo/2015/05/21/moat-and-mediaocean-partner-to-help-marketers-buy-viewable-ads/"for being one of the leading companies that track viewability determining whether online ads can be seen by real people. But the 125-person company, which had previously raised over $17 million, aspires to do much more. According to co-founder and Chief Executive Jonah Goodhart, Moat is working on a new measurement designed to help Web advertisers and publishers gauge whether people pay attention to digital ads and are impacted by them.

The investment round was led by Insight Venture Partners.

The ultimate goal for Moat will be to have advertisers negotiate ad deals using this new metric, rather than relying on the long-standing practice of billing advertisers based on how many ads are delivered to a Web page by  ad serving" software.

Typically, ad serving data comes from Google the leading ad serving provider in the industry. That data is often used by media companies to track how many ads they deliver and to bill advertisers. In addition, advertisers often use tools from comScore and Nielsen to enhance ad serving data, such as tracking the demographic makeup of the audience that is exposed to an ad campaign.

But as more digital advertising moves away from standard banners on Web pages toward video and native ads on platforms like YouTube, Snapchat and Facebook, Moat sees an opportunity to displace Google and other metrics providers.

Mr. Goodhart said Moat is already well positioned to do this, since it has its own viewability tracking mechanisms plugged into "https://www.facebook.com/business/news/updated-features-for-video-ads""http://marketingland.com/youtube-opens-up-to-third-party-viewability-verification-150208"and Twitter. Plus, a major advertiser, Unilever, is already transacting ad deals using Moat data.

 Our ambition is to build the currency for digital advertising," he said, while emphasizing that Moat sees companies like "http://www.nielsen.com/us/en/solutions/capabilities/digital-ad-ratings.html"and Google as potential partners in this effort.

Moat has successfully pivoted before. The company first launched with the idea of building a crowdsourcing tool for online ad creative, which never took off. Over time, the Moat team built a "http://www.moat.com/"ads, then started selling access to advanced analytics to marketers.

In the past few years, as the "http://blogs.wsj.com/cmo/2015/01/27/the-push-for-web-ad-viewability-proving-to-be-nightmare-for-publishers-early-on/" to start only buying ads that are viewable, Moat was in the right place at the right time and moved aggressively to build out viewability tracking tools for both desktops and mobile Web pages.

While he declined to discuss specific figures, Mr. Goodhart said that Moat has been profitable for the past two years and that revenue tripled from 2014 to 2015.

 The first thing I wrote down when we started this company five years ago was, we need to make brand advertising more effective online," said Mr. Goodhart. He and his Moat partners Noah Goodhart and Michael Walrath had previously partnered to create Right Media, an early digital ad exchange that was heavily used by direct-response advertisers. It was "http://www.reuters.com/article/us-rightmedia-yahoo-idUSN3037037520070430".

With Moat, the plan was to cater more to the kinds of marketers who spent heavily on TV, like car, beer and toothpaste brands.  Most stuff is bought offline," he said.  We're trying to connect those dots. If you look at TV, it has a currency [with Nielsen data]. It may be flawed, but the point is, everyone is on the same page."

Surely, most every digital media company would like to see more TV ad budgets come their way. But getting everyone on the same page for a brand new currency won't be easy. Just getting viewability standards set across the industry has been challenging.

Peter Naylor, head of sales for Hulu, said he started working with Moat a few years ago when brands started pushing the viewability concept. That move led to more ad budgets coming Hulu's way, he said.

So he's impressed with Moat's technology and approach.  They are really in touch with the needs of both sides of the business," he said.  There is an opening there. But [ad] server counting is pretty foundationally sound in the industry."

Megan Pagliuca, global chief executive at Accuen, an agency that specializes in data-driven ad buying, said she's generally on board with Moat's philosophy.  I do think the industry needs to evolve beyond the metrics we have today," she said.  Ad serving data is clearly not enough."

But Ms. Pagliuca said it may be hard for any one company to solve all of the digital ad world's needs. For example, besides tracking whether ads are viewable or command attention, advertisers are looking for help in tracking people across different devices and different media vehicles, she said.

For now, Moat is focused on building out its technology aimed at developing this potential new ad currency, as well as hiring. The plan is to double staff this year, Mr. Goodhart said.  We think we are at an interesting crossroads in the industry," he said.  Where Marketers are saying,  if I can't measure it, I can't buy it.'"

Jefferies suffers 50% fall in revenues

Ben McLannahan in New York 
577 words
15 March 2016
13:30
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Jefferies & Co foreshadowed a grim set of first-quarter results for the world s biggest investment banks on Tuesday, posting a 50 per cent collapse in net revenues and a  humbling  quarterly loss.

The firm s November year-end, and its trading and advisory focus, means that it has long served as a curtainraiser for the biggest banks on Wall Street, which report figures about a month later. So far, senior executives at banks such as Citigroup have prepared investors for a particularly gloomy period, noting big year-on-year falls in revenues from M&A and capital-raising and much weaker trading.

Jefferies, which is privately owned by Leucadia, said that its first quarter net revenues tumbled across the board, from capital markets (-28 per cent) to asset management (-20 per cent). But the carnage was most apparent in sales and trading, where revenues in fixed-income were down 55 per cent and in equities by more than 99 per cent.

That pushed the firm to a rare quarterly loss of $167m for the period between December and February, down from a profit of $12m a year earlier.

Rich Handler, the firm s chairman and chief executive officer, said that the results reflected  an exceptionally volatile and turbulent market environment  throughout January and the first few weeks of February.

 Almost every asset class 	. 	. 	. 	suffered significantly amid concerns about the pace of global economic growth, outflows from the high yield market, forced selling from hedge funds, uncertainty over China, a potential Brexit, and an overall void in liquidity,  he said.

The bleak first-quarter for the big banks is likely to highlight basic flaws in the investment banking model, where falls in revenues are not met with commensurate falls in expenses. Jefferies  net revenues for the period came to $299m, but it paid $350m in compensation and benefits.

George Kuznetsov, London-based head of research and analytics at Coalition, said that the first quarter for the big investment banks is traditionally strong, as renewed activity by clients normally shows  businesses in a much better light. 

 This quarter it has been exactly the opposite,  he said. Simultaneous slumps in revenues across debt, equity and advisory units suggests  there is no benefit at all in portfolio diversification. 

Jefferies said that its near-total collapse in equities revenues was attributable primarily to a $145m markdown related to two listed equity block positions, one of which was KCG Holdings, a market-maker and high-frequency trader it helped rescue in 2012. KCG, formerly known as Knight Capital Group, hit trouble after flooding the market with unintended trades, prompting a $400m sale of convertible shares in a deal arranged by Jefferies.

Mr Handler urged patience on Tuesday, saying that Jefferies had a  significant long-term opportunity  to gain market share as rivals refocus on retail and commercial banking.

Investment-banking and trading activities have looked brighter recently, Jefferies said, and a big improvement in results   including mark-ups of the KCG position   seemed likely in the current period ending in May.

 While we are early in the second quarter and one can never predict the future, it appears markets have not only stabilised but aggressively snapped back,  said Mr Handler.

 We are humbled by Jefferies' quarterly loss and will strive to deliver the better results that our shareholders deserve and Jefferies is more than capable of achieving,  he said.

COMPANIES
High street innovator looks to gain ground in digital push; Interview: Ashok Vaswani

By Emma Dunkley 
798 words
15 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
23
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

New head of Barclays UK plans to put lender's online offering at front of agenda

Voice recognition, 3D printers and robots serving bank customers evoke the image of a futuristic digital society.

For Barclays' Ashok Vaswani , this digital vision is becoming a reality, and is starting to ripple through the UK's banking sector.

Digital developments are at the fore of 55-year-old Mr Vaswani's agenda in his new role as chief executive of Barclays UK, one of two divisions recently created to drive the future of the banking group.

In its annual results this month, Barclays unveiled a radical restructure, basing the business on two core units comprising the "ringfenced" bank in Britain - Barclays UK - and corporate and international banking.

The move puts Mr Vaswani in charge of consumer and business banking, wealth management and the domestic credit cards business, Barclaycard UK.

"It's the first time we've brought the entire product set together," says Mr Vaswani in his first interview since the new banking structure was unveiled.

"Deepening our relationships is critical to our entire strategy."

Underpinning this goal is Mr Vaswani's focus on employing digital technology to "make customers' lives much easier".

He points out that the bank's "most digitally advanced product" is consumer lending. Barclays' instant lending via mobile, for example, preapproves customers using the bank's data to tailor loans which are then visible on mobile and obtained within six taps.

But Mr Vaswani is also keenly aware of increasing competition in this area and the need to keep costs in check. He sees digital as a way of "pushing through more volume."

While net income remains broadly flat at 8.7bn compared with 2013, just before the retail bank in its current guise was created, costs are coming down. The retail bank's cost/income ratio now sits at 60 per cent, down from 68 per cent in 2013.

Like other banks, Barclays is closing a number of branches within its 1,350-strong network, including three this month, as the number of customers visiting is in perpetual decline.

The bank now has 4.8m customers using mobile banking, who log on about 28 times a month on average, more than three years since the app was launched.

Investors believe the retail bank is a high-quality asset for the group and welcome the new combined UK division.

Rob James, an analyst at Old Mutual Global Investors, says: "It makes a high return - in common with most retail banks - at about 16 per cent, which means that it is capable of both paying a good dividend to the group and also growing its balance sheet when opportunities arise.

"When you put this business together with Barclaycard you have a real gem of a core business."

Barclays took another 1.45bn hit in the fourth quarter for past mis-selling of payment protection insurance , dragging the banking group into a loss in the final three months of the year.

Ian Gordon, an analyst at Investec, says the retail bank is "a decent, conservatively positioned business making adequate returns, but with weak growth". Unlike other high street banks, he says Barclays does not have a material number of existing mortgage customers paying higher rates, putting the bank under pressure in a low interest rate environment.

However, he believes Barclays is "generally well ahead of peers in terms of technological advance, and offering further scope for cost-efficiency improvement."

While Barclays has pioneered a number of innovative digital products such as "Pingit", its person-to-person mobile payments service, Mr Vaswani is alert to other banks developing superior digital capabilities.

"I'm paranoid . . . I look at big banks, I look at challenger banks. I look at everybody, to stay ahead of the game."

Royal Bank of Scotland revealed this month that it is launching artificial intelligence to interact with staff, to help them assist their small business customers. Mr Vaswani says Barclays is at present experimenting with the technology before launching any AI-based services for consumers.

Mr Vaswani also has grand plans for becoming the bank for entrepreneurs, and is hoping to launch a blueprint over the coming months. The bank is combining its wealth, entrepreneur and business banking customers together into one division - which it dubs "webb" - with a focus on serving high-growth companies.

The launch of Barclays' Eagle labs nationwide, which provides space within certain branches for businesses to use digital equipment from laser cutters to 3D printers, is aimed at helping local companies grow and to win new customers.

"Eagle labs are where we are going to unleash the potential that digital empowerment brings," Mr Vaswani says.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

English
Copyright  2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Citic's Swap Is a Step Forward

China's state-run enterprises may not always reform the way investors want them to. But, as an asset swap between two of China's largest such companies shows, they can still better each other's lives.

Citic, the country's oldest state-run conglomerate, said Monday that it would unload almost all of its residential property to China Overseas Land and Investment, one of the largest state property developers. COLI would pay $4.8 billion, not including the value of debt. In exchange, Citic receives a 10% stake in COLI, plus $950 million worth of commercial property.

On the whole, this deal is to Citic's advantage. Given the conglomerate's sprawl from iron ore to a soccer team, handing over property to a company whose sole job is to manage this asset class makes sense.

Citic, whose real-estate and infrastructure arm clocked an annualized 1.68% return on assets in the first half of 2015, was never the best manager. In contrast, COLI posts returns on assets above 8%, with returns on equity above 20%. For Citic to own shares in a company with high returns is a big improvement. The downside is that Citic isn't receiving cash, which it could have deployed wherever it wanted, especially in the services sector. The conglomerate previously said that it needs to "embrace" China's shift from heavy industry to consumption. Owning shares in a property developer doesn't exactly fit that billing.

Citic records an immediate book-value gain of up to $1.4 billion on selling the assets. But that only partially expiates the sins of some other investments. Last week, Citic said it would reduce the book value of its Australian iron-ore project by up to $1.7 billion thanks to falling commodity prices. It took a similar hit last year.

COLI looks like it is smartly boosting scale during a downturn. It is enlarging its land bank by 60%, putting it far above its rival China Vanke's, according to data from Standard & Poor's. It helps that most of the new land COLI is getting is in tier-one or tier-two cities, Morgan Stanley says, where demand is more stable.

As for the price tag of 1.6 times book value, this could be misleading. Chinese developers don't revalue their land banks unless they turn over assets, says Macquarie's David Ng. Citic hasn't been doing so, meaning the market value of this land is almost certainly higher, and that COLI isn't paying such a hefty premium.

Both COLI and Citic shares rallied on news of the deal. Citic, though, still trades a quarter below Citigroup's estimate of its net asset value. This gap will only materially narrow when Citic does more than swap assets and starts shedding troubled business lines.

-- Abheek Bhattacharya

China Vanke Goes Back to Its Roots With Defensive Bid

Takeover tussles are rare in China. And in an unusual defensive move, the world's largest real-estate developer may fend off its unwelcome suitor by returning to the embrace of its original state owner.

China Vanke said Sunday that it has signed a memorandum of understanding with Shenzhen Metro to buy property assets fromthe subway operator, which is fully owned by the municipal government of this southern Chinese city. The deal size is around 40 billion to 60 billion yuan ($6.16 billion to $9.23 billion), mostly financed by issuing new shares. Vanke was founded by the Shenzhen government about three decades ago. The municipal government has since sold its entire stake.

The move is likely Vanke's attempt to unseat its largest shareholder, Baoneng Group, which owns 24% of the company after aggressively buying up Vanke's shares last year. Such hostile situations are unusual because Chinese companies generally have big controlling shareholders. But Vanke's senior management and staff only own around 5% of the company.

Vanke hasn't disclosed at what price the new shares will be issued. Using the price of its Hong Kong shares before the deal's announcement, Shenzhen Metro could get between 24% to 35% of the developer. State-controlled shareholders, including China Resources Group which is friendly to Vanke's management, would be left owning around 40% of the developer, assuming the potential deal is approved by regulators and shareholders.

Vanke's shares in Shenzhen make up the majority of the company's issued shares, but they have been haltedfrom trading since December, pending the announcement of an asset restructuring. The shares in Hong Kong continue to trade since each exchange has its own regulations.

Shenzhen Metro has around 4.5 million square meters in salable gross floor area in Shenzhen, arguably the hottest property market in China right now. The potential transaction would nearly triple Vanke's land bank in Shenzhen, according to Goldman Sachs.

Vanke's shareholders in Hong Kong already like the partnership, sending its shares up 10%Monday. The stock has fallen 20% since December as investors worry that Vanke may take extreme measures, like a "poison pill," to dilute Baoneng's shareholding. Vanke has yet to announce the pricing for the transaction, but Shenzhen Metro has some good assets. This could prove a good deal for both parties.

Vanke's chosen pill doesn't look that poisonous.

-- Jacky Wong

Qualcomm's

New iPhone

Chip Angst

Intel's mobile efforts have been long stymied by the fact that it has remained shut out of the most profitable smartphone on the market: the iPhone.

That may be changing. Rumors have circulated that Intel may grab market share from Qualcomm for the modem chip, or baseband processor, handling the iPhone's connections to wireless networks. Morgan Stanley, Cowen andCredit Suissethis week allnoted signs that production of a new Intel chip is gearing up ahead of the expected iPhone 7 launch in the fall.

Less clear is whether the smaller iPhone that Apple reportedly plans to unveil at a March 21 event also would make use of these chips. And it is worth noting that rumors of an Intel chip in the iPhone have circulated in regard to prior generations of the smartphone.

Qualcomm controls about 60% of the baseband market, according to Strategy Analytics. So it makes sense for a major buyer like Apple to seek out a second source on a key component. But analysts say Qualcomm is highly unlikely to lose the entire iPhone business, since its baseband chips still are superior to the competition.

Stacy Rasgon of Bernstein estimates that losing 30% of the Apple business would cut about 7% off Wall Street's consensus earnings projection for Qualcomm's 2017 fiscal year. But Wall Street already projects a 14% decline in Qualcomm'schipset revenuefor the current fiscal year. With the stock already down 28% over the past 12 months, Qualcomm investorsmay be pricing ineven bleaker prospects.

-- Dan Gallagher

Overheard

To swap or not to swap, that is the question.

China's central bank is preparing new regulations that would make it easier for banks to convert theirbad loans into borrowers' equity. Such swaps are common for creditors to maximize the value of their soured loans, but China currently restricts banks from investing in nonfinancial companies. By giving the troubled companies breathing space on their debt, the lenders could eventually enjoy the upside from their revival.

Creditors of shipbuilder-turned-oil-explorer China Huarong Energy may be the first to try, having agreed to swap loans into equity last week. But its shipbuilding business may not turn around in the near future because of relentless oversupply. As for oil, China Huarong ventured into an oil field in Kyrgyzstan in 2014, right when prices began falling.

For Huarong's creditors, swapping into its shares is just prolonging the pain.

Chinese Group Bids for Starwood

By Joshua Jamerson 
809 words
15 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
A1
English
Copyright  2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Starwood Hotels & Resorts Worldwide Inc. has received a $12.8 billion takeover offer from a group led by China's Anbang Insurance Group Co., threatening to upend a pending deal with Marriott International Inc.

Anbang's move is the latest by Chinese companies looking to snap up properties in the U.S., as they view high-quality real estate as a haven for savings.

The Anbang consortium is offering to pay $76 a share in cash for all shares outstanding of the company. That is a 7.9% premium to Starwood's closing stock price of $70.42 on Friday. Marriott's cash-and-stock deal is valued at $65.38 a share, or about $11 billion, based on Friday's closing price.

In announcing the new bid, Starwood said its board hadn't changed its recommendation in support of the merger with Marriott, but that it is permitted to talk with rival bidders until March 17.

Starwood didn't name members of the group that made the offer. Marriott identified Anbang as the leader of the rival consortium, while saying it was still committed to its deal for Starwood.

A representative from Anbang declined to comment. Starwood couldn't be reached for comment.

Marriott noted that if Starwood were to terminate the deal, it would owe Marriott a $400 million termination fee. Shareholders of each company are scheduled to vote on the deal on March 28.

Shares of Starwood rose 6.9% to $75.27 in late-morning trading in New York. Marriott shares rose 2% to $70.26.

The owner of the Westin and Sheraton brands among others, Starwood said it received the rival offer on March 10 and began discussions with the consortium a day later, after it received a waiver from Marriott allowing it to engage in discussions.

Chinese investment firm Primavera Capital Group and J.C. Flowers & Co. are also part of the group making the offer, people familiar with the matter said. The companies couldn't be reached for comment.

Marriott agreed to acquire Starwood in November. The deal would create the No. 1 hotel company globally -- with more than one million rooms -- and bring together 30 brands across all segments of the loding industry, from Starwood's higher-end W Hotels and St. Regis brands to Marriott's limited-service offerings like Courtyard by Marriott and its extended-stay chain Residence Inn.

Chinese buyers have pushed to buy U.S. hotel properties lately. Asian buyers in recent years have acquired New York's Plaza Hotel and Carlyle Hotel at prices that exceeded $1.4 million a room, according to hotel data tracker STR Analytics.

Anbang struck a deal in October 2014 to buy Hilton Worldwide Holdings Inc.'s flagship hotel, the historic Waldorf Astoria in Manhattan, for $1.95 billion.

Anbang is also near a deal to buy Strategic Hotels & Resorts Inc. from a Blackstone-managed real-estate fund, people familiar with the situation said. That deal would give Anbang a substantial presence in luxury hotels across the U.S. The price Anbang is paying couldn't be determined.

Once a provincial car insurer, Beijing-headquartered Anbang has leapt onto the global stage by making several high-profile deals. The company has spent billions purchasing part or all of insurers in South Korea, Europe and the U.S., as well as taking stakes in listed Chinese developers, a bank, a maker of traditional Chinese medicine and a wind-turbine manufacturer.

Primavera Capital Group is a China-based private-equity firm founded by Chinese deal maker Fred Hu, the former chairman for Greater China at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. A prominent investment banker and economist, Mr. Hu led Goldman's $2.9 billion deal to take a 5% stake in Industrial & Commercial Bank of China Ltd. Mr. Hu has built ties with a range of China's most important financial figures.

Primavera was also an investor in Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. before its initial public offering as part of a financing package raised to repurchase half of Yahoo Inc.'s stake in Alibaba. Mr. Hu's role in that deal and close relationship with Alibaba Executive Chairman Jack Ma won Primavera a coveted investment in Alibaba affiliate Ant Financial Services Group, China's most valuable Internet finance company, last year.

Chinese companies have done more than $84 billion in deals since the start of the year, according to Dealogic, setting them up to exceed the record $108 billion of Chinese outbound acquisitions reached last year. Fueling these takeovers are relatively loose regulations that allow insurers, funds, trust companies and others outside the formal banking sector to issue higher-yielding trust and other investment products.

---

Dana Mattioli and Rick Carew contributed to this article

Goldman Sachs has extended its push into the financial technology sector on Monday with the acquisition of Honest Dollar, a Texas-based startup that helps small businesses set up retirement saving plans for staff.

Honest Dollar is a web-based service that s tapping demand from the roughly 45m Americans who do not have access to employer-sponsored retirement plans. It s designed to make it easier for small and medium-sized companies, self-employed individuals and independent contractors to establish individual retirement account (IRA)-based savings plans, the bank said on Monday.

 Honest Dollar has created a simple solution to a complex retirement savings problem,  said Timothy J. O Neill and Eric S. Lane, executives at Goldman said.  Together, we have the potential to help millions of people achieve their investing goals. 

Honest Dollar claims on its website that employers can sign up as quickly as in 90 seconds while costs start as little as $8 per employee per month. Terms of the deal were not disclosed.

The deal marks Goldman s latest foray into fintech. The bank has invested in bitcoin startup Circle Internet Financial, payment technology provider Bluefin Payment and data analytics startup Kensho.

THE MONDAY INTERVIEW
Mindful of mortality; Monday interview: Mark Bertolini, Aetna CEO

By David Crow 
1,439 words
14 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The unconventional boss of the US health insurer distrusts hospitals and business school 'poison', writes David Crow

When Mark Bertolini's son, Eric, was struck by a rare type of cancer at the age of 16, his doctors said to prepare for the worst. "Forty-seven people had been diagnosed with the disease before, all of them men between the ages of 16 and 34, and they were all dead," he recalls.

Mr Bertolini, now chief executive of Aetna, the third-largest US health insurer, refused to accept Eric's prognosis. He found an oncologist in Boston who was willing to try an experiment - a "bad" bone marrow transplant that would kill the cancerous cells.

The transplant wiped out the cancer but Eric's body rejected the bone marrow and he became extremely sick. "He went from 140lb to 80lb in a matter of months," says Mr Bertolini. "He was starving."

It was then that Mr Bertolini quit his job as an insurance executive at Cigna and moved into his son's hospital room to manage his care personally.

He downloaded a copy of Harrison's Principles of Internal Medicine , a bible for junior doctors, and started having fierce arguments with the medics, who thought he was in denial about his son's chances of survival.

By 2003, Eric had made a full recovery, in part because his father had convinced regulators to let him take an unapproved medicine. Today, Eric works at State Street, the asset manager.

Mr Bertolini returned to work with radically different views about the US healthcare system. "Lesson one was that they always viewed him as the lymphoma in room four, whereas I knew him from the delivery room when he was born," he says.

"Their view of him was as a disease not a person."

Eric's story is remarkable - not least because his father ended up giving him a kidney in 2007, following complications resulting from his cancer treatment.

The reality of dealing with health insurers in the US tends to be less uplifting, however. Aetna achieved only a middling ranking in JD Power's consumer satisfaction table in 2015. Customers tend to prefer not-for-profit health plans offered by rivals such as the Kaiser Foundation.

Mr Bertolini, however, believes his personal experience can be applied to his vast employer, which has a market capitalisation of $38bn and 23m health insurance customers.

He points to three changes he has made since taking the top job in 2010. Aetna now encourages people to travel to secure the best treatment, as Eric did. "People tend to want to be near home but when they travel for more than two hours to get specialised care, they have much higher survival rates," says Mr Bertolini; the insurer has dropped a requirement that patients cease curative treatment before being admitted to a hospice; and it is collecting anonymised genetic profiles of members to glean "big data" insights into which drugs work best for which patients.

His son's illness has informed Mr Bertolini's approach to running one of the larger US companies - which is poised to get even bigger with its pending $37bn acquisition of rival group, Humana - as has a horrific accident of his own.

In February 2004, a year to the day after his son was discharged, Mr Bertolini smashed into a tree while skiing in Vermont, breaking his neck and back, injuring his head and damaging nerves. When he regained consciousness a week later, he found that he was alive only because he had fallen headfirst into a freezing river, which acted like an ice-pack on his spinal cord.

His distrust of hospitals, which he says are "full of diseases", kicked in. He discharged himself as soon as he could, clutching "all sorts of narcotics", including the opioid painkillers that are handed out so liberally in the US.

Despite being sceptical about alternative treatments, he found relief in cranio-sacral therapy, which some doctors have dismissed as quackery. "I was a total doubter," he recalls.

"Somebody's going to hold on to my rear end and my head, and I'm going to feel better? Well, what the hell - I was all hopped up on drugs."

Three sessions in, he says he started to feel better; three months later he stopped taking the painkillers.

Unable to go on his daily run, Mr Bertolini turned to yoga, where he met his partner Mari, a yogi who trained in Chennai, India, in the 1980s. He quickly developed a passion for "the whole Hindu view of the world," and he has tried to put this into practice at Aetna, which offers yoga and mindfulness sessions to staff.

"Our chief medical officer said, 'this is voodoo don't you know'," says Mr Bertolini. "And I said, 'well let's do a study'." More than 13,000 Aetna employees have since had a 12-week stint of mindfulness or yoga, and Mr Bertolini says it has resulted in "a dramatic increase in productivity".

He eschews the management texts beloved by other chief executives and accuses American business schools of "poisoning the economic thinking of every businessman in the world", even though he has an MBA in finance from Cornell University. He also decries "these things called spreadsheets".

Instead, he turns to Hindu texts such as the Upanishads and the Bhagavad Gita for insights, as well as writings by Abraham Maslow, the late humanist psychologist, and Capital in the Twenty-First Century , the seminal book on income in-equality by French economist Thomas Piketty, published in 2013.

It was Mr Piketty's book that encouraged Mr Bertolini to implement a company-wide pay increase last year, which set a $16 an hour minimum for all Aetna employees, equivalent to a one-third pay rise for some of its lowest paid workers.

Mr Bertolini took home $15m in pay, bonus and long-term incentive award in 2014, the last year for which figures have been disclosed, on top of $31m in 2013.

Aetna has also improved its staff medical plan, which dramatically reduced the amount that low-paid employees had to contribute to their healthcare. "When I went out into the field to shake everybody's hands, I kept hearing the same thing: 'I can't afford the healthcare, I'm working two jobs, my family's on food stamps,'" he says.

Mr Bertolini says the stories reminded him of his childhood in Detroit, where his father worked in the auto industry and his mother was a part-time nurse.

"I too grew up in a family that didn't have health insurance at times, that was on government programmes for cheese and bread and milk," he recalls. "It wasn't a bad life, but today it's harder."

The Humana deal is expected to face tough scrutiny from antitrust authorities, amid claims that it will reduce competition and drive up costs.

Mr Bertolini thinks the opposite is the case, that Aetna's size and its assured profitability will give him the freedom to experiment.

"While I may be a little different as a CEO, in how I think about how to invest and play the game, I am very, very focused on making sure we deliver on our results," he says. "Because that's what gives us the ability to do the rest."

On his wrist, Mr Bertolini sports an expensive Swiss watch, a staple for many chief executives, and a nod to the conventional ways of big business. But he also wears two unusual black rings, one in the shape of a skull.

He tells colleagues it reminds him that "time is short".

CV

Born June 7, 1956, Detroit

Education Undergraduate degree in business administration/finance from Wayne State University in Detroit; MBA in finance from Cornell University

Career

1985-2003: Various positions at SelectCare (where he was president and chief executive), NYLCare Health Plans and Cigna

2003-present: Joins Aetna, initially as head of speciality products, before moving up to president in 2007; CEO since November 2010; chairman and CEO since April 2011

Interests Motorcycling, yoga, Hindu theology, urban gardening

Second opinion

The fellow CEO

Lowell McAdam, CEO of telecom company Verizon, values the healthcare experience Mark Bertolini brings as a member of its board. "[It] has provided a unique and valuable perspective [on] an area of increasing importance to Verizon's strategy. We share his vision for creating a healthier world, and Mark has demonstrated true leadership in efforts to create innovative healthcare solutions now and in the future."

Corrections & Amplifications

The loss of 30% of Apple Inc.'s modem chip business could cut about 7% off Wall Street's projection for Qualcomm Inc.'s per share earnings for the 2017 fiscal year, according to Stacy Rasgon of Bernstein. A Heard on the Street column Monday about the mobile chip market incorrectly said the change would be for the company's current fiscal year.

(WSJE March 15, 2016)

(END)

Qualcomm's

New iPhone

Chip Angst

Intel's mobile efforts have been long stymied by the fact that it has remained shut out of the most profitable smartphone on the market: the iPhone.

That may be changing. Rumors have circulated that Intel may grab market share from Qualcomm for the modem chip, or baseband processor, handling the iPhone's connections to wireless networks. Morgan Stanley, Cowen and Credit Suisse last week all noted signs that production of a new Intel chip is gearing up ahead of the expected iPhone 7 launch in the fall.

Less clear is whether the smaller iPhone that Apple reportedly plans to unveil at a March 21 event also would make use of these chips. And it is worth noting that rumors of an Intel chip in the iPhone have circulated in regard to prior generations of the smartphone.

Qualcomm controls about 60% of the baseband market, according to Strategy Analytics. So it makes sense for a major buyer like Apple to seek out a second source on a key component. But analysts say Qualcomm is highly unlikely to lose the entire iPhone business, because its baseband chips still are superior to the competition.

Stacy Rasgon of Bernstein estimates that losing 30% of Apple's business would cut about 7% off of consensus profit projections for Qualcomm this fiscal year. But Wall Street already projects a 14% decline in Qualcomm'schipset revenuefor the current fiscal year.

With the stock already down 28% over the past 12 months, Qualcomm investors may be pricing in even bleaker prospects.

-- Dan Gallagher

ECB Wakes Up to Bank-Profit Nightmare

Mario Draghi is worried about eurozone bank profits after all.

The European Central Bank president said in January that it wasn't his job to protect banks and on Thursday insisted that loose policy had so far helped not hindered their profits. But the ECB's big policy moves contained special tricks designed to protect banks against the debilitating effects of negative interest rates.

Investors were initially confused: Bank shares rallied then fell back on Thursday. But bank stocks then rose sharply Friday, especially those of Southern European banks, as the implications became clear.

Negative rates are a nightmare for banks. They have to pay to leave money on deposit at the central bank and can end up paying to own government bonds. At the same time, banks can't pass negative rates to retail depositors either because they fear the money will leave, or in some countries because they aren't allowed to by law.

Bank stocks and bonds have been heavily sold by investors this year because of worries about the sector's profitability and the effects on dividends and coupon payments for some junior bonds.

Mr. Draghi acknowledged Thursday that the pain caused to banks over the longer term would put a limit on how far negative he could push interest rates. To protect banks and stop them from cutting back lending, the ECB launched more targeted lending operations, known as TLTRO II. These offer banks more than 700 billion euros ($780 billion) of fresh four-year funding, according to Barclays, to back their loans to companies and consumers, but not mortgages.

At most this will cost banks nothing. But if they have grown lending by 2.5% by the end of January 2018, the interest rate they pay could drop to the ECB's deposit rate at the time the funding was taken. That was cut to minus 0.4% on Thursday.

This funding is a cheap option to replace banks' maturing bonds with more than 500 billion euros due for repayment in the next two years, according to Standard & Poor's, as well to refinance existing ECB money. The lower costs would add a net 3 billion euros, or up to 2.5%, to annual earnings, according to Deutsche Bank.

Mr. Draghi closed his news conference Thursday with a very deliberate reference to a private European Commission paper on ways to ease costs on an important element of bank capital. The commission isn't releasing the paper, but one of its aims is to suggest more leeway for banks to avoid skipping coupon payments on junior debt known as additional tier-one capital.

Fears about missed coupons were behind the selloff in these junior bonds this year, which infected share prices and senior bonds, too.

These moves are having the desired effect in the markets. Two indexes that measure the riskiness of senior and subordinated European bank bonds dropped sharply. The ECB won't solve the bank profitability problem like this, but at least it is trying to stop making it worse.

-- Paul J. Davies

The New Test of Success for the European Central Bank

Time to switch focus. Until now, the euro's exchange rate has been closely tracked as an indicator of the effectiveness of the European Central Bank's stimulus programs. Now eurozone corporate bonds are the crucial barometer investors need to watch.

Thursday's decision to add corporate bonds to the ECB's purchase programs marks a shift in President Mario Draghi's strategy. Previously, much of the focus was on lowering the government-bond yield curve, which had the side effect of delivering a weaker euro both to help growth via exports but also boost inflation via higher import prices. Now credit markets have joined the party, which means corporate-bond spreads -- the gap between yields on company debt and underlying government bonds -- are of vital importance.

This is new territory for the ECB. The Bank of England briefly engaged in corporate-bond purchases in early 2009, but total holdings only reached GBP 1.6 billion ($2.3 billion) and the focus swiftly shifted to buying gilts in huge quantities. The ECB is also buying at a different stage in the cycle: The BOE moved when financial markets were truly in disarray and corporate-bond yields were very high. Now the credit cycle is already fairly advanced, although less so in Europe than in the U.S.

Many questions remain about how the ECB will go about its corporate-bond purchases. It is unclear how much of the extra 20 billion euros ($22.3 billion) a month of central-bank cash announced Thursday will go into credit markets. The potential universe of eligible company debt remains to be determined too, although strategists peg it at roughly 500 billion euros.

But there is already some evidence of the power of ECB buying. Some Italian utilities, such as Enel and Terna, got onto the ECB's shopping list last year, and their bonds have sharply outperformed the wider market since then, Bank of America Merrill Lynch notes.

There are pitfalls too. The success of the program will ultimately depend on companies' appetite for investment. And the ECB's largess in lowering the overall cost of borrowing in Europe has led toa rush of euro-denominated bond issuance by U.S. companies. Last year, they accounted for nearly one-quarter of issuance and so far this year for a third, Societe Generale notes. Some of the ECB's efforts may just contribute to more debt building up on U.S. investment-grade balance sheets.

The spread on BAML's European corporate-bond index has already narrowed to 1.43 percentage points from a February peak of 1.63 points. The cost of insuring European company debt against default has fallen very sharply since the ECB decision was announced; cash bonds are slower to move, but there is substantial room for a rally from here.

How far it goes, and how much it stirs the animal spirits of company executives, will determine how effective the ECB's latest gambit proves to be.

-- Richard Barley

Mark Bertolini, Aetna CEO: mindful of mortality

David Crow 
1,266 words
13 March 2016

Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

When Mark Bertolini s son, Eric, was struck by a rare type of cancer at the age of 16, his doctors said to prepare for the worst.  Forty-seven people had been diagnosed with the disease before, all of them men between the ages of 16 and 34, and they were all dead,  he recalls.

Mr Bertolini, now chief executive of Aetna, the third-largest US health insurer, refused to accept Eric s prognosis. He found an oncologist in Boston who was willing to try an experiment   a  bad  bone marrow transplant that would kill the cancerous cells.

The transplant wiped out the cancer but Eric s body rejected the bone marrow and he became extremely sick.  He went from 140lb to 80lb in a matter of months,  says Mr Bertolini.  He was starving. 

It was then that Mr Bertolini quit his job as an insurance executive at Cigna and moved into his son s hospital room to manage his care personally.

He downloaded a copy of Harrison s Principles of Internal Medicine, a bible for junior doctors, and started having fierce arguments with the medics, who thought he was in denial about his son s chances of survival.

By 2003, Eric had made a full recovery, in part because his father had convinced regulators to let him take an unapproved medicine. Today, Eric works at State Street, the asset manager.

Mr Bertolini returned to work with radically different views about the US healthcare system.  Lesson one was that they always viewed him as the lymphoma in room four, whereas I knew him from the delivery room when he was born,  he says.  Their view of him was as a disease, not a person. 

Eric s personal story is remarkable   not least because his father ended up giving him a kidney in 2007, following complications resulting from his cancer treatment.

The reality of dealing with health insurers in the US tends to be less uplifting, however. Aetna achieved only a middling ranking in JD Power s annual consumer satisfaction table in 2015. Customers tend to prefer not-for-profit health plans offered by rivals such as the Kaiser Foundation.

Mr Bertolini, however, believes his personal experience can be applied to his vast employer, which has a market capitalisation of $38bn and 23m health insurance customers.

He points to three changes he has made since taking the top job in 2010. Aetna now encourages people to travel to secure the best treatment, as Eric did.  People tend to want to be near home but when they travel for more than two hours to get specialised care, they have much higher survival rates,  says Mr Bertolini; the insurer has dropped a requirement that patients cease curative treatment before being admitted to a hospice, and it is collecting anonymised genetic profiles of members to glean  big data  insights into which drugs work best for which patients.

His son s illness has informed Mr Bertolini s unconventional approach to running one of the larger US companies   which is poised to get even bigger with its pending $37bn acquisition of rival group, Humana   as has a horrific accident of his own.

In February 2004, a year to the day after his son was discharged, Mr Bertolini smashed into a tree while skiing in Vermont, breaking his neck and back, injuring his head and damaging nerves. When he regained consciousness a week later, he found that he was alive only because he had fallen headfirst into a freezing river, which acted like an ice-pack on his spinal cord.

His distrust of hospitals, which he says are  full of diseases , kicked in. He discharged himself as soon as he could, clutching  all sorts of narcotics , including the opioid painkillers that are handed out so liberally in the US.

Despite being sceptical about alternative treatments, he found relief in craniosacral therapy, which some doctors have dismissed as quackery.  I was a total doubter,  he recalls.

 Somebody s going to hold on to my rear end and my head, and I m going to feel better? Well, what the hell   I was all hopped up on drugs. 

Three sessions in, he says he started to feel better; three months later he stopped taking painkillers.

Unable to go on his daily run, Mr Bertolini turned to yoga, where he met his partner Mari, a yogi who trained in Chennai, India, in the 1980s. He quickly developed a passion for  the whole Hindu view of the world,  and he has tried to put this into practice at Aetna, which offers yoga and mindfulness sessions to staff.

 Our chief medical officer said,  this is voodoo, don t you know ,  says Mr Bertolini.  And I said,  well let s do a study .  More than 13,000 Aetna employees have since had a 12-week stint of mindfulness or yoga, and Mr Bertolini says it has resulted in  a dramatic increase in productivity .

He eschews the management texts beloved by other chief executives and accuses American business schools of  poisoning the economic thinking of every businessman in the world , even though he has an MBA in finance from Cornell University. He also decries  these things called spreadsheets .

Instead, he turns to Hindu texts such as the Upanishads and the Bhagavad Gita for insights, as well as writings by Abraham Maslow, the late humanist psychologist, and Capital in the Twenty-First Century, the seminal book on income inequality by French economist Thomas Piketty, published in 2013.

It was Mr Piketty s book that encouraged Mr Bertolini to implement a company-wide pay increase last year, which set a $16 an hour minimum for all Aetna employees, equivalent to a one-third pay rise for some of its lowest-paid workers.

Mr Bertolini took home $15m in pay, bonus and long-term incentive award in 2014, the last year for which figures have been disclosed, on top of $31m in 2013.

Aetna has also improved its staff medical plan, which dramatically reduced the amount that low-paid employees had to contribute to their healthcare.  When I went out into the field to shake everybody s hands, I kept hearing the same thing:  I can t afford the healthcare, I m working two jobs, my family s on food stamps,   he says.

Mr Bertolini says the stories reminded him of his childhood in Detroit, where his father worked in the auto industry and his mother was a part-time nurse.

 I too grew up in a family that didn t have health insurance at times, that was on government programmes for cheese and bread and milk,  he recalls.  It wasn t a bad life, but today it s harder. 

The Humana deal is expected to face tough scrutiny from antitrust authorities amid claims that it will reduce competition and drive up costs.

Mr Bertolini thinks the opposite is the case, that Aetna s size and its assured profitability will give him the freedom to experiment.

 While I may be a little different as a CEO, in how I think about how to invest and play the game, I am very, very focused on making sure we deliver on our results,  he says.  Because that s what gives us the ability to do the rest. 

On his wrist, Mr Bertolini sports an expensive Swiss watch, a staple for many chief executives, and a nod to the conventional ways of big business, but he also wears two unusual black rings, one in the shape of a skull.

He tells colleagues it reminds him that  time is short .

Intel Considers Sale of Some Venture Capital Assets to Refocus Resources; Sale would allow company to focus on startups in key technology, geographic areas

By Deborah Gage 
441 words
11 March 2016
23:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SANTA CLARA Intel Capital is exploring the sale of part of its venture capital portfolio to focus on the areas that are most strategic to Intel, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The sale, which may not occur, comes from a shift in strategy initiated by Intel Chief Executive Brian Krzanich, who was named to the post in May 2013 when former CEO Paul Otellini retired, the person said.

News of a potential sale was first reported by Bloomberg.

Under Mr. Otellini, Intel Capital was one of the most active venture investors in the world, investing globally in a variety of areas to expand the market for PCs running Intel chips. Since 1991, Intel Capital has invested more than $11.64 billion in 1,440 companies in 57 countries.

Now, however, Intel's challenges are different than they were even three years ago when Mr. Krzanich took over. Mobile devices are overtaking PCs, and cloud computing, security, enterprise software, data centers and the Internet of Things are becoming increasingly important technologies.

A sale could give Intel Capital more resources to focus on startups in those key technology areas and on geographies like the U.S. and China that are especially important to Intel, the person said, although the assets being considered, which could add up to a $1 billion, are a small portion of Intel's total portfolio.

In 2014, for instance, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702304157204579473400192410782" in Cloudera Inc., which at the time was marketing a rival version of the big data software Hadoop. Intel set its own version of Hadoop aside. In 2015, Intel led a $100 million investment in Mirantis Inc., which sells subscriptions to the open-source cloud software OpenStack.

Intel has been considering the potential sale since at least the middle of last year, hiring UBS AG to explore a sale of some assets in India, the person said.

But nothing happened because Intel Capital was preparing for a leadership change, with former Intel Capital President Arvind Sodhani getting ready to retire and current Intel Capital President Wendell Brooks getting ready to take over, and the potential sale was viewed as a distraction.

Intel declined to comment on the moves. In a blog post in February, Mr. Brooks said Intel Capital would continue to invest $300 million to $500 million a year in startups  across the full spectrum of technologies where Intel excels."

Shares of Intel climbed after the news, closing at $31.76, up 51 cents.

Markets
What Crisis? Big Ratings Firms Stronger Than Ever; S&P, Moody s and Fitch issue more than 95% of global bond ratings, and profits are nearing all-time highs

By Timothy W. Martin 
1,032 words
11 March 2016
17:06
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The three big ratings firms that played a central role in the last financial crisis never got a downgrade of their own.

Investors still overwhelmingly rely on Standard & Poor's Ratings Services, Moody's Investors Service and Fitch Ratings when deciding whether to buy bonds. The three issue more than 95% of global bond ratings, a total virtually unchanged from the pre-2008 period.

Profits also are nearing all-time highs as they ride a recent wave of debt sales and push into new lines of business.

The resilience of the industry's largest players was on display again this week as Moody's, a unit of Moody's Corp., "http://www.wsj.com/articles/moodys-to-pay-calpers-130-million-to-settle-lawsuit-1457554312" with a California pension fund. The pact resolved one of the industry's last remaining major crisis-related legal headaches and brought the total of fines and settlements to $1.9 billion, a fraction of the amount paid by U.S. banks for missteps during the same period.

 The credit-rating agencies got away so easy, given what they did," said Marcus Stanley, policy director at Americans for Financial Reform, a nonpartisan coalition in favor of stronger Wall Street regulation.  The happy days are here again. There's not really been an interruption of the profit flow."

The fallout from the financial crisis was supposed to crimp the credit-ratings model. Firms awarded rosy ratings to residential mortgage bonds that later soured, triggering widespread losses.

Lawmakers and regulators called for a major shake-up of the way the firms made their money. But seven years later, the industry's business blueprint in which banks and debt issuers still pay ratings firms to have their deals graded remains in place despite concerns about its potential conflicts.

 Things remain 80% as dangerous as they were in 2007 and 2008," said U.S. Rep. Brad Sherman (D., Calif.), who proposed in 2009 that an independent board assign deals to ratings firms. A proposal was inserted into the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul bill but never implemented.

 The notion that nothing has changed in this industry is pure fiction," said Daniel Noonan, a spokesman for Fitch, jointly owned by Fimalac SA and Hearst Corp.

A spokesman for S&P, a unit of McGraw Hill Financial Inc., said the firm in recent years has enhanced ratings methodology, revised analytics and created a new global committee to identify and monitor risks. Moody's declined to comment.

There were multiple efforts by upstarts to challenge the big three firms, but all fell flat. One, Kroll Bond Rating Agency Inc., tried unsuccessfully to persuade investors to pay for the ratings before eventually switching back to the old model used by its larger rivals.

Other attempts sputtered out before they could get going. Chinese firm Dagong Global Credit Rating Co. had its application denied in 2010 over disagreements about how it would be vetted. No new ratings firm has registered with the Securities and Exchange Commission since HR Ratings de Mexico SA of Mexico City did in 2012.

 Systemic change is not happening," said James H. Gellert, chief executive of Rapid Ratings International Inc., a firm that gets paid by investors, not debt issuers.

Ratings firms said they have addressed concerns that emerged in the wake of the crisis by beefing up compliance and risk departments charged with making sure business interests don't sway bond grades. They also have new oversight from an SEC division created to police the industry.

All three are less reliant on ratings revenue after expanding their businesses into the collection of broader financial data.

The mortgage-backed bond that helped fuel the housing-market collapse also represents a smaller portion of overall revenue. In the run-up to the crisis, ratings firms pocketed about half of their revenue grading deals involving securitized mortgages or other bonds. Now, that total is closer to 10%.

But the ratings firms are now so large, some observers said, that their removal would cause significant damage to the financial markets. Investors use ratings when buying fixed-income securities.

 They're a necessity," said Philip Hilder, a Houston-based lawyer and a former federal prosecutor.  They are a part of the market. It's not feasible or imaginable to dismantle them or destroy their reputation. You couldn't just give them a fatal blow."

Moody's isn't out of the clear. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/moodys-acknowledges-currency-headwinds-as-profit-rises-14-1423225316" and the Justice Department are scrutinizing the firm's behavior before the crisis, according to people familiar with the matter.

The investigation is similar in scope to one that resulted in the more than $1.3 billion penalty paid by S&P last year.

Both probes center on whether the firms issued inflated ratings that misrepresented the risks of mortgage-related deals. Under its 2015 settlement with the Justice Department, S&P admitted that it kept issuing positive ratings even though it recognized problems with the securities.

There are other signs that scrutiny of the ratings firms is more aggressive now. In January 2015, the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/s-p-sec-two-states-agree-to-roughly-80-million-settlement-1421851682" to win business and mislead investors long after 2008. Andrew Ceresney, the SEC enforcement director, said the regulator's findings at S&P  reflect a deep cultural failure at S&P and a failure to learn the lessons of the financial crisis."

S&P didn't admit or deny wrongdoing, paying a $77 million settlement. It said at the time that it was pleased to have concluded the federal and state probes and  takes compliance with regulatory obligations very seriously."

The industry  may be better at the margins, but it is still fundamentally structurally flawed," said Phil Angelides, former California state treasurer who led the bipartisan Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission, which included a case study of Moody's in its 2011 report.  It's a simple fact."

Aruna Viswanatha contributed to this article.

"http://www.wsj.com/pro/centralbanking/graphics/what-ecb-qe-stimulus-has-done/"

FRANKFURT The European Central Bank fired off a salvo of measures aimed at bolstering the eurozone's fragile economy but markets brushed off the efforts, raising questions about whether it and other central banks still have the tools to bolster weakening growth and inflation after years of easy-money policies.

Across the advanced world central banks are under pressure to launch fresh monetary stimulus or keep interest rates low in response to volatile financial markets, falling oil prices and a slowdown in emerging markets.

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/bank-of-japan-introduces-negative-interest-rates-1454040311"in January by setting the country's first negative interest rates in an attempt to jolt the economy away from deflation. Federal Reserve officials are "http://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-almost-certain-to-keep-interest-rates-unchanged-at-next-meeting-1452882343" at their policy meeting next week and leave open-ended as to when they will next raise rates.

Financial "http://www.wsj.com/articles/euro-weakens-european-stocks-rise-on-ecb-stimulus-measures-1457616212"in response to the ECB's larger-than-expected stimulus on Thursday, which included a series of rate cuts, additional bond purchases and ultracheap loans for banks. But investors' initial enthusiasm over the stimulus measures faded when, afterward, ECB President Mario Draghi signaled that interest rates probably wouldn't fall any lower amid concerns about the impact on Europe's fragile banks.

 They gave the market a gift and then took it away within an hour," Eddie Perkin, chief equity investment officer at Eaton Vance Management, said.

It was the second time in three months the ECB ramped up its stimulus in an effort to bolster the eurozone's persistently weak inflation and the second time markets appeared unimpressed.

The euro seesawed, falling as low as $1.082 in response to the new stimulus before recovering during the ECB's news conference and spiking toward $1.12. European stocks ended sharply lower, headlined by a 2% slide in Germany's benchmark DAX. An initial government-bond rally in Spain, Portugal and Italy fizzled. Even in the U.S., markets reacted negatively.

The ECB's latest move  will probably simply continue to benefit primarily the financial markets but do little to alter low growth and low [underlying] inflation," said Joerg Kraemer, chief economist at Commerzbank.

The fresh measures included cuts to all three of the ECB's main interest rates,  ¬20 billion a month of"http://www.wsj.com/articles/ecb-announces-stimulus-plan-1421931011"atop the ECB's current  ¬60 billion ($67 billion) program, and an expansion of its quantitative easing program to highly rated corporate bonds all more aggressive steps than analysts had anticipated. The central bank also announced a series of ultracheap four-year loans to banks, some of which could be paid to borrow from the ECB.

Early on, investors cheered the array of moves.  This has all the hallmarks of the ECB having thrown the kitchen sink at the problem," said Aberdeen Asset Management Investment Manager Patrick O'Donnell.

At a news conference, Mr. Draghi said the stimulus was a response to  significantly changed" conditions in the global economy and financial markets since the ECB's policy meeting in December. At that meeting, the ECB disappointed investors with a smaller-than-expected package of measures that included a 0.1 percentage-point cut to its deposit rate and a six-month extension of its bond purchases.

The fresh stimulus was partly a response to new quarterly economic forecasts, published by the ECB on Thursday, which showed growth and inflation likely falling sharply below the central bank's December projections. It now expects an inflation rate of just 0.1% this year, down from 1% projected in December and far below its target of near 2%.

The ECB has missed its inflation objective for three straight years despite launching its massive bond-purchase program a year ago. The Bank for International Settlements, a central bank for central banks, warned on Sunday that central-bank stimulus may have less traction in financial markets than it once had.

Mr. Draghi brushed off those concerns.  We have shown that we are not short of ammunition," he said.

Undermining that claim, though, Mr. Draghi said the ECB didn't currently anticipate cutting rates any further out of concerns about the impact on banks. Subzero rates act as a tax on banks because they can't easily pass on the costs to customers.

 Does it mean that &we can go as negative as we want without any consequences on the banking system? The answer is no," he said. Those comments helped send the euro soaring against the dollar, and stock and bond markets sprawling.

 Markets seem unsure what to make of the ECB's action, as many may believe that the bank has reached the limits of central-bank policy-making and will run out of ammunition in the future," said Barbara Teixeira Araujo, an economist at Moody's Analytics.

Even if the ECB rejects further rate cuts, Mr. Draghi still has a number of bullets left. The ECB could accelerate its bond purchases further, or extend their likely end-date beyond March 2017, moves that some economists expect later this year. To step up the bond-buying even more, Mr. Draghi could change the program's design by skewing toward the bonds of highly-indebted governments such as Italy, or lifting bond-issue and issuer limits, currently at 33% for most bonds. He could also expand into other assets such as bank debt, or even stocks and real estate.

The ECB decided not to introduce a multi-tier system for its deposit rate, which would impose the highest charges on only a portion of banks reserves, as practiced by central banks in Japan and Switzerland.

Such a system could allow rates to fall even further below zero, but Mr. Draghi said the ECB had decided against it  exactly for the purpose of not signaling that we can go as low as we want." He said its emphasis would shift away from interest rates and toward other policy tools, such as bond purchases.

In that vein, the ECB said it would accelerate its monthly bond purchases to  ¬80 billion from  ¬60 billion, which adds  ¬240 billion to the overall program, and start buying highly-rated corporate bonds in an effort to avoid bottlenecks. It also rolled out a series of four-year loans for banks, whose interest rate can range from zero to minus 0.4%, meaning that the ECB will pay some banks to borrow money if they meet criteria on net lending to the economy.

The loans are designed to help  further ease credit conditions and stimulate credit creation," and will help eurozone banks deal with  sizable upcoming funding needs," Mr. Draghi said.

Nick Kounis, an economist at ABN Amro in Amsterdam, estimated that the takeup from banks could be up to  ¬1.25 trillion. Mr. Draghi said he expected  very sizable" demand.

The ECB also reiterated its guidance, saying that interest rates would remain at current or lower levels for an extended period. Mr. Draghi stressed that while it might take longer than expected to reach its objective,  this doesn't mean we have deflation." He said the ECB's actions in recent years had helped avert  disastrous deflation."

But economists said that given the sharp deterioration in the outlook, the ECB may have to announce fresh stimulus soon.  We suspect that the ECB's work is still not done," said Jonathan Loynes, an economist at Capital Economics in London.

COMMENT
Good news - fintech could disrupt finance

By Martin Wolf on Economics 
949 words
9 March 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
11
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Information technology has disrupted the entertainment, media and retail businesses and, most recently, the supply of hotel rooms and taxis. Is it going to do the same to finance? My first response is: please. My second response is: yes. As Bill Gates has said, "We always overestimate the change that will occur in the next two years and underestimate the change that will occur in the next 10. Don't let yourself be lulled into inaction." This advice applies to people in the business itself, but also to policymakers.

Finance is an information business. Indeed it already spends a higher share of its revenues on information technology than any other. It seems ripe for disruption by information technologies. Consider its three essential functions: payment; intermediation between savings and investment; and insurance. All these activities are informationintensive. People need to know accounts have been settled. They need to understand how their wealth is being employed and to know that their risks are covered. Not least, the intermediaries need to understand what they are doing.

Today, banks and insurance companies are the core financial institutions. Banks manage payments systems, create most of the economy's money, are responsible for a large proportion of financial intermediation, are creators of financial instruments and act as market-makers and agents. Similarly, insurance companies play the central role in assessing and managing risks.

Why might one hope that new financial technology, or " Fintech " as it is known, will transform these businesses? The answer, especially for banking, is that they are currently not done very well. Banking seems inefficient, costly, riddled with conflicts of interest, prone to unethical behaviour, and, not least, able to generate huge crises.

In a recent speech on the possibilities for a financial revolution, Andrew Haldane of the Bank of England notes that, astonishingly, the unit cost of US financial intermediation seems to be unchanged over a century (see chart). Moreover, income from finance simply rises and falls with the value of assets. That suggests a huge amount of rentextraction. Additionally, 10m US households and 1.5m UK adults still have no bank accounts. Worldwide, banks generate a staggering $1.7tn in revenue, 40 per cent of the total, from the job of making payments. In the computer age, settlement can still take hours or days.

On behaviour, as John Kay has written, "parts of the financial sector today . . . demonstrate the lowest ethical standards of any legal industry". The payment of vast fines seems to be viewed as just a cost of doing business. Finally, the post-2007 banking crises were as big as any in the past. That their economic impact was not still worse than earlier was due to the willingness of governments to bail banks out.

New technology might help change this in at least two ways. First, it might transform payments. One possibility is real-time settlement via distributed ledgers. The advantages of instantaneous settlement are evident. The advantage of distributed ledgers, an element in bitcoin's "blockchain" technology, is an improvement in the robustness of record-keeping. Instead of centralised accounts, the database would be shared across a network of sites, all of which would hold an identical copy. Such technologies might revolutionise domestic and foreign payments. Many businesses are already pursuing this possibility.

A second transformation might be via peer-to-peer lending, in which new platforms disintermediate the traditional businesses in matching savers with investments. Such lending is growing rapidly (see chart). The theory here is that computerised information might allow savers to dispense with the (costly) services of bankers altogether.

Optimists imagine a future in which payments, the creation of money (unquestionably liquid and safe assets), and intermediation would be separated. In this case, the capacity of the banking sector to create havoc would be reduced and so would the perils created by the state's backstop to private institutions. It is, however, far too early to be confident of such benefits. Indeed it is easy to see that new record-keeping and payments systems would create huge security issues. Similarly, opportunities for malfeasance also exist on peer-to-peer platforms. Indeed, these are inevitable with transactions that rest on promises against an inherently uncertain future.

A further potential source of transformation is via "big data". That might transform the quality of lending, for example, which would be a good thing. But the most striking effects are likely to be in insurance. With new monitoring devices, insurers might gain direct knowledge of the quality of driving or of the state of their clients' health. Such information might be used to motivate improvements in behaviour. But it is also possible to imagine improvements in information so profound that risk pools - the basic building blocks of insurance - disappear. If, for example, the insurer knew with a high level of certainty that some customers would get a given disease, that person might become uninsurable. In insurance, some ignorance is bliss. At the least, the way in which knowledge is obtained and used could create huge social questions.

On balance, the opportunities afforded by the application of information technologies to our financial system seem large. The difficulty might rather be to ensure that the benefits accrue this time to the public rather than to a small number of incumbents or even to their more dynamic replacements. Finance, particularly banking, does need a revolution. But this is one area where policymakers cannot just assume things will work out well. It is because finance is so important that a revolution is needed. But for that very reason the revolution also requires careful watching.

martin.wolf@ft.com

The Property Report: Investors Target Students in U.K.

By Art Patnaude 
550 words
9 March 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B6
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

International investors are piling into U.K. student housing, with a flurry of deals showing renewed interest in this small corner of the property market.

Greystar Real Estate Partners of the U.S. has agreed to buy a student-housing portfolio for about GBP 600 million ($856 million) from U.K. private-equity group Round Hill Capital LLC, a person familiar with the deal said.

Last week, the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board spent GBP 1.1 billion to acquire the U.K.'s Liberty Living, while a company headed by Russian oligarch Mikhail Fridman paid GBP 532 million for another London student-housing portfolio.

Yields on office buildings and other traditional property types have fallen as investors flush with cash and able to borrow at low rates have pushed prices higher. That has some investors looking elsewhere for higher returns.

Traditionally, real-estate investing "has been focused around the main commercial property types of retail, office, and industrial," a February report from the Investment Property Forum, a U.K. industry body, said.

But investors' hunt for higher long-term returns is producing "significant changes in the market," said Jon Neale, head of U.K. research at broker JLL. The fastest-growing sectors are now those deemed "alternatives" -- like student-housing, hotels, health care and privately rented residential real estate -- GBP 11.6 billion of which traded in 2014, a 33% increase from 2013, JLL figures show.

It remains a small part of the GBP 65 billion of U.K. real estate traded last year, but alternatives "accounted for only a few percentage points of volumes just a few years ago," Mr. Neale said.

For U.K. student accommodation, the average investment yield has been 5.8% this year, compared with 5.1% on offices, according to Real Capital Analytics data.

The yield on student housing is down from 6.4% last year, a reflection of rising prices.

There were about $2.4 billion of student housing transactions in the U.K. in 2014, down 18% from 2013, according to Real Capital Analytics. U.S. firms Greystar, LaSalle Investment and Goldman Sachs have been among the biggest buyers in the last two years, the data show.

Last Friday, the Canadian Pension Plan bought 16,700 student rooms in more than 40 residences across 17 cities -- as well as the company that manages the business -- from the Brandeaux Student Accommodation Fund.

This was the Canadian investor's largest-ever real-estate deal in Europe, and its first there in the alternative space, said Andrea Orlandi, head of European real-estate investments.

For CPP, student accommodation provides "a noticeable pickup in the yield" offered by office investments, Mr. Orlandi said.

The opportunity to buy the management company and its team means, "we can look to buy more assets" in a sector "that can, and will, consolidate in the future," he said.

In the Greystar deal Monday, Round Hill is selling three London residences with 2,375 beds, a portfolio it bought for $415 million from Blackstone Group in 2012. Round Hill will retain the student-housing brand Nido, its management business and one student residence in London it bought earlier this year.
