Economy
Fed Could Face Political Blowback if It Ever Went Negative on Rates; Policy could come under fire if it were to hurt retail depositors, Federal Financial Analytics  paper says

By Kate Davidson 
576 words
23 February 2016
21:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Federal Reserve may have the legal authority to push interest rates into negative territory, but the politics of such a decision may prove to be a thornier challenge.

That's the conclusion of a new report from Federal Financial Analytics Inc., a Washington policy-analysis firm, which says the Fed will face significant blowback from Capitol Hill if the strategy appeared to hurt retail and small-business depositors.

Fed officials are unlikely to engineer negative interest rates any time soon. They are now focused on whether and when to raise rates in coming months. But they have said they could consider pushing rates below zero if they needed to provide more stimulus during an economic downturn, and the policy is generating interest as other central banks experiment with it.

In a 2010 internal memo, the Fed contemplated a maneuver to push rates into negative territory by charging banks interest rather than paying interest on reserves they deposit with the central bank. But that likely would lead banks to charge depositors  for providing the equivalent of cash-vault services," and could create a political maelstrom for the Fed, according to the Federal Financial Analytics report.

 Charging depositors transforms negative IOER (interest on excess reserves) from a financial-policy challenge into a grassroots political one, especially given the current U.S. focus on income inequality," the paper argues.

On the other hand, the Fed likely has  very little room" to try to resurrect the previous tools it has used to stimulate the economy, given the intense opposition it has faced from Congress in recent years, the paper said. Lawmakers on both sides of the aisle have also recently taken aim at the interest the Fed pays to big banks.

Negative interest rates,  either through negative IOER or another channel has the possible advantage of being seen as a tax on banks appealing across the political spectrum if politicians are persuaded that community banks are largely exempt from it," the paper said.

In testimony before Congress this month, Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen said the central bank is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/yellen-reiterates-concerns-about-risks-to-economy-in-senate-testimony-1455203865" if needed to give the economy a boost.  We are taking a look at them again," she told the Senate Banking Committee.

She said she didn't think the Fed would need to push rates below zero, but it should be prepared to do so if the economy sinks and such a step is needed.

Central banks in Europe and Japan have turned to the once-radical idea of negative interest rates to spur their moribund economies. Many economists believe the U.S. central bank is unlikely to follow suit Ms. Yellen said the Fed is unlikely to lower rates any time soon, never mind going negative but Fed officials are taking the idea seriously. They recently required banks to model how they would perform under negative borrowing costs as part of its annual bank  stress tests."

The political and popular backlash to Japan's decision to take rates negative  is a strongly-cautionary test case for the [Fed], but not one that we believe will dispositively dissuade it from NIRP [negative interest-rate policy] if nothing else seems to."

On Deck Reports Growing Revenue, But Losses Mount; Operating expenses rose 73% in the fourth quarter

By Peter Rudegeair 
425 words
23 February 2016
01:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

On Deck Capital Inc. reported record revenue and loan volume in the fourth quarter, but it wasn't enough to drive the online small business lender into profitability.

OnDeck's shares fell 20% to $7.02 in aftermarket trading, though volume after the close was only around 1% of the daily average over the past 65 trading sessions.

The company on Monday reported a net loss of $5.1 million for the final three months of 2015, more than the $4.3 million it reported losing in the same period the year before. On an adjusted earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, or Ebitda, basis, a measure of profitability that excludes some costs such as interest payments and taxes, OnDeck reported net income of $315,000, less than the $561,00 it reported in the same prior-year period.

Gross revenue in the fourth quarter increased 34% to a record $67.6 million, more than the company told investors to expect three months ago. OnDeck had to spend more to drive that growth, though, as operating expenses increased 73% due to higher spending on sales and marketing and technology and analytics.

OnDeck executives said it planned to invest more in technology to secure more partnerships similar to the one it announced with J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. in December. The company said that deal doesn't preclude others with large banks but that it wouldn't expect the initiative to add much to revenue before 2017.

 We're confident these investments will fortify our leadership position," OnDeck finance chief Howard Katzenberg said on a conference call with analysts.

Much of the higher revenue came from OnDeck selling a record amount of its loans to institutional investors. The company sold $201.9 million of loans, or 40% of the record $557 million it originated in the fourth quarter, through its marketplace.

While On Deck books a revenue gain from such sales, it foregoes receiving interest payments on those loans. The effective yield on OnDeck's outstanding loans also slipped in the fourth quarter, leading overall interest income to fall 2% compared with the third quarter.

Concerns about a potential recession have contributed to the 15% fall in OnDeck's shares since the start of the year, but Chief Executive Noah Breslow said the company isn't currently seeing any weakness in its loan quality.

The worrying signal from US online lending

Kadhim Shubber 
987 words
22 February 2016
12:10
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

In recent months, both Lending Club and Prosper Marketplace have hiked the rates they charge their borrowers. The conversation about this has largely focussed on what it says about online lending. Have they been underwriting badly and so now are scrabbling to account for past mistakes? Are they having to offer higher returns to investors who are tempted to put their money elsewhere? Is this the sign of stress that will bring the whole digital house of cards tumbling down?

Forget all of that for a moment and suppose that Lending Club or Prosper loans are just like any other consumer loan. Now ask yourself this question: what do the actions of those lenders say about the US economy itself? The answer isn t re-assuring.

Lending Club"http://www.wsj.com/articles/lendingclub-raises-interest-rates-for-new-loans-following-fed-move-1450818060", raising rates by 25 basis points shortly after the Fed hiked in December. It hiked again in January and is now charging borrowers 47 basis points more on average than it was in November. But as Tracy Alloway "http://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2016-01-29/when-credit-market-concerns-arrive-at-the-marketplace-lenders",  that average conceals a bifurcation: Rates on loans deemed higher quality by the company ticked down 3 bps, while rates on lower-quality ones rose by about 67 bps. 

Prosper was slower. Last week, it finally raised rates but it did so "http://www.wsj.com/articles/prosper-marketplace-boosts-interest-charge-on-its-consumer-loans-1455743138", with rates jumping an average 140 basis points. But again, rates on the safer credit rose relatively little, while rates on the riskier credit rose quite a bit. Rates on the loans Prosper rates as AA or A rose just 20 basis points, according to an email sent to investors. Rates on the C, D, E and HR [high-risk] loans rose 70, 130, 180 and 130 basis points respectively.

So what are Lending Club and Prosper reacting to?

Maybe late last year both companies hired a new underwriting whizz who came in and said,  oh jeez you re doing this all wrong. Rates are way below where they should be. Hike you fools!  Another explanation is that they are having to do more to persuade investors to buy their loans purely on a competitive basis i.e. they re trying to make their loans attractive relative to other assets.

But maybe something about their borrowers changed.

Ram Ahluwalia, chief executive of risk analytics platform PeerIQ, tracks credit across the online lending sector. He has seen  delinquencies uptick mildly in the [second half] of 2015 .

 All things being equal, credit risk is vintage specific, and the most recent vintage has a somewhat higher delinquency rate than prior vintages,  he says, with lower-FICO loans experiencing a 2 or 3 times bigger rise than higher-quality credit.

This isn t a massive blowout, it s an uptick, but it s there nonetheless, even when seasonality and geography is taken into consideration, and it s across the consumer loan originators PeerIQ tracks. And again the uptick is higher for riskier borrowers than it is for less risky borrowers.

Moody s has also spotted something going on with the performance of online-originated loans. Earlier this month it put three bonds backed by Prosper loans and sold by Citigroup on "https://www.moodys.com/research/Moodys-places-on-review-for-downgrade-marketplace-lending-ABS-issued--PR_343705". The move was prompted  by a faster buildup of delinquencies and charge-offs than expected in transactions backed by Prosper-originated loans . Around the same time Moody s also "https://www.moodys.com/research/Moodys-upgrades-marketplace-lending-ABS-issued-by-Consumer-Credit-Origination--PR_343675" its loss assumptions for two Blackrock bonds, also backed by Prosper loans.

Brian Weinstein, a former Blackrock fixed income guy who is now chief investment officer at Blue Elephant Capital Management, has a bearish thesis about all of this: people who have borrowed from online lenders are finding it harder to pay their bills now than they were this time last year.

Blue Elephant buys loans from the likes of Prosper and Funding Circle, typically higher quality credit. Weinstein says that defaults have been ticking up since about August last year and that they have moved  rapidly higher  for the lower quality credit. This, he says, is why Lending Club and Prosper have raised rates. And it s why the rates have jumped up so much faster for riskier borrowers than for less risky borrowers. There will be more hikes to come, he argues, as the companies respond to the deterioration in credit.

 I think it s very simple. If you look at our book, [for] the higher quality borrower, the default rates have moved slightly higher. If you look at the lower quality stuff, the default rates have moved rapidly higher. 

The conclusion he draws is that there is  a legitimate economic slowdown that s affecting the lower credit quality borrowers in the United States .

That s all assuming that there isn t just something going wrong with online lending in particular. It s possible   perhaps this is a false signal and we should be more worried than we already are about the ability of this new crop of companies to underwrite.

But consider that the business models of Lending Club and Prosper is to pass-through loans direct from borrowers to institutional investors (largely) and remember that there is more attention on these companies and their credit than most. It s not inconceivable that they might respond faster and more publicly to changes in credit because they are deeply sensitive to any suggestion that they are asleep at the steering wheel.

It s one we ll be keeping our eye on. Perhaps it s a blip. But perhaps it s indicating a real slowdown in growth.

We ll leave you with this thought from Weinstein on the lenders who don t have the name-recognition of Prosper and Lending Club.

 I ve seen some of the loan tapes from other competitors  & I ve seen the guys who take the rejects and the performance is horrific

UK COMPANIES
City Insider

Edited by Patrick Jenkins 
596 words
19 February 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Peter Clarke

Good hedge

Peter Clarke is hedging his bets. The old Man Group boss, who came unstuck after poor performance in 2012, has reinvented himself with a portfolio career. Last year he joined the board of Essentia Analytics, a fintech business that uses software to help fund managers. He's also been working as a non-exec at a couple of asset managers - Axa, where he chairs the UK investment outfit, and Lombard Odier. Now he has been made chairman of Lancashire Holdings, the Lloyd's insurer. His appointment coincided with weak results from Lancashire. The trend might bring back bad memories of Man in 2012. But for Lancashire chief Alex Maloney , Clarke is just the man to have around. "You learn a lot more when things go wrong than when they go right," he told City Insider. Hold tight!

Dan Rosenfield

Going underground

Mandarins are not always the politically neutral figures they are supposed to be. Testament then to the broad appeal of Dan Rosenfield , who was principal private secretary to two successive chancellors, Alistair Darling and George Osborne, and even had both speak at his leaving do in 2011. Whether he's quite so popular in Whitehall these days is another question. During his subsequent five years as an investment banker with Bank of America Merrill Lynch, his highest profile mandate was advising on the Royal Mail privatisation. That was a triumph for investors who bought in at 330p and were selling within weeks at nearly 600p. Not so much for his old paymasters. Now Rosenfield is moving on again - this time to private investigator outfit, Hakluyt. By all accounts, the mandarin turned banker is a jolly nice chap. But BAML executives will still be praying he doesn't repeat the trick of turning on his previous employer, however accidentally.

Rory Tapner

Cold comfort

While markets slide, Rory Tapner , the former CEO of Coutts, is rounding up the great and the good from the investment world to complete a slippery descent of their own. Tapner is scouring the market for teams to take part in the Coeur Blanc ski challenge in France's Mribel. Richard Gower , friend to cocoa trader Anthony "Chocfinger" Ward and co-founder of their firm Armajaro, will be among those trying to forget the flashing red of their trading terminals by riding 52 lifts in one day. And just to prove fat cats have hearts, all teams will be raising money for Snow-Camp, a charity for disadvantaged teenagers. Tapner says it's a test of endurance, not a race. Nothing to do with the fact that five times Olympic gold medallist Sir Steve Redgrave is heading a rival team.

Patrick Spens

Lord Blockchain

PwC is going big on the blockchain. This week it signed an alliance with Digital Asset Holdings, the start-up headed by Blythe Masters , the former JPMorgan investment banker. Now the consulting firm is hiring Patrick Spens , latterly of the Financial Conduct Authority, to study the "transformational potential of blockchain". The appointment rounds off PwC's hiring of 15 blockchain specialists last month. The firm reckons Spens - a Citigroup trader and a hedge fund manager, before in his own words going to "do his bit" at the regulator, policing insider trading - is just the man to spread the blockchain mantra among clients. City Insider has been unable to confirm suggestions that the hereditary peer, who 15 years ago became the fourth Baron Spens of Blairsanquhar, also answers to the title Lord Blockchain.

MARKETS & INVESTING
IBM shake-up efforts rewarded with best rally in nearly 7 years; Wall Street

By Adam Samson 
505 words
19 February 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
21
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

IBM was on track to post its best rally in almost seven years after Morgan Stanley said the markets had "under-appreciated" Big Blue's transformation efforts.

The computer group has seen its sales fall every year since 2012 as its legacy businesses, such as making servers and business workstations, have deteriorated amid a shift by many companies to host their data and processing operations in shared data centres in the cloud.

Its shares have struggled, too, falling more than 18 per cent over the past 12 months before yesterday's rally.

Chief executive Ginni Rometty has sought to steer the more than century-old technology company toward higher-growth opportunities with investment in "strategic imperatives", which include a move into cloud services, data analytics, mobile user engagement and cyber security.

Strategic imperatives revenues rose 17 per cent last year to $28.9bn, representing 35 per cent of the company's total sales. But that failed to offset declines in other legacy businesses.

Katy Hubert, an analyst at Morgan Stanley, reckoned that IBM's revenue growth was now bottoming, with sales returning to growth by 2019 as strategic imperatives become more than half IBM's total sales.

She upgraded the stock from "equal-weight" to "overweight".

Ms Hubert also flagged IBM's investment in analytics services as perhaps more important than its cloud push amid stiff competition from companies such as Amazon in the cloud sector.

For instance, IBM hopes its Watson super computer service that crunches large amounts of data will have broad-ranging impacts on many businesses, ranging from healthcare to retail.

As part of that plan, the company yesterday unveiled a $2.6bn buyout of a healthcare data company that is meant to bolster its health data and analytics division.

IBM's shares rose 6.3 per cent to $134 in morning trading in New York yesterday, in what would be its biggest one-day advance since March 2009, according to Bloomberg data.

Elsewhere, Walmart shares came under pressure after the world's biggest retailer posted its first annual sales decline since at least 1980.

The company has struggled under competition from ecommerce companies, such as Amazon, as well as the impact of the strong US dollar on its foreign sales.

Walmart also dimmed its sales forecast for this fiscal year from growth of between 3 and 4 per cent to flat.

The shares dropped 4.5 per cent to $63.13, extending their 12-month decline to 27 per cent.

Jack in the Box , a US fast-food chain, meanwhile, fell 15.6 per cent to $64.86 after the company posted weaker-than-expected quarterly earnings.

In midday trading, the S&P 500 fell 0.3 per cent to 1,920.3, the Dow Jones Industrial Average shed 0.2 per cent to 16,422.3 and the Nasdaq Composite dipped 0.6 per cent to 4,505.7.

Careers: Bosses Tap Big Data  To Flag Workers' Ills

By Rachel Emma Silverman 
1,029 words
18 February 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Employee wellness firms and insurers are working with companies to mine data about the prescription drugs workers use, how they shop and even whether they vote, to predict their individual health needs and recommend treatments.

Trying to stem rising health-care costs, some companies, including retailer Wal-Mart Stores Inc., are paying firms like Castlight Healthcare Inc. to collect and crunch employee data to identify, for example, which workers are at risk for diabetes, and target them with personalized messages nudging them toward a doctor or services such as weight-loss programs.

Companies say the goal is to get employees to improve their own health as a way to cut corporate health-care bills.

Privacy advocates have raised concerns about such practices. Employees generally have a choice in whether to participate in the programs. The services are new enough that relatively few workers are aware of them.

"I bet I could better predict your risk of a heart attack by where you shop and where you eat than by your genome," says Harry Greenspun, director of Deloitte LLP's Center for Health Solutions, a research arm of the consulting firm's health-care practice.

An employee who spends money at a bike shop is more likely to be in good health than someone who spends on videogames, Dr. Greenspun says.

Credit scores can also suggest whether an individual will be readmitted to the hospital following an illness, he says. Those with lower credit scores may be less likely to fill prescriptions and show up for follow-up appointments, adds Dr. Greenspun.

Welltok Inc., whose clients include Colorado's state employees, has found that people who vote in midterm elections tend to be healthier than those who skip them, says Chris Coloian, the firm's chief solutions officer.

In general, midterm voters are more mobile and more active in the community, strong indicators of overall health, he says.

As employers more actively involve themselves in employee wellness, privacy experts worry that management could obtain workers' health information, even if by accident, and use it to make workplace decisions.

Federal health-privacy laws generally bar employers from viewing workers' personal health information, though self-insured employers have more leeway, says Careen Martin, a health-care and cybersecurity lawyer at Nilan Johnson Lewis PA. Instead, employers contract with wellness firms who have access to workers' health data.

"There are enormous potential risks" in these efforts, such as the exposure of personal health data to employers or others," says Frank Pasquale, a law professor at the University of Maryland, who studies health privacy.

Typically, when a company hires a firm like Castlight, Jiff Inc. or ActiveHealth Management Inc., it authorizes the firm to collect information from insurers and other health companies that work with the client company. Employees are prompted to grant the firm permission to send them health and wellness information via an app, email or other channels, but can opt out.

Based on data such as an individual's claims history, the firms can identify an individual who might be considering costly procedures like spinal surgery, and can send that person recommendations for a second opinion or physical therapy.

Spinal surgery, which can cost $20,000 or more, is another area where data experts are digging in. After finding that 30% of employees who got second opinions from top-rated medical centers ended up forgoing spinal surgery, Wal-Mart tapped Castlight to identify and communicate with workers suffering from back pain.

To find them, Castlight scans insurance claims related to back pain, back imaging or physical therapy, plus pharmaceutical claims for pain medications or spinal injections. Once identified, the workers get information about measures that could delay or head off surgery, such as physical therapy or second-opinion providers.

Some firms, such as Welltok and GNS Healthcare Inc., also buy information from data brokers that lets them draw connections between consumer behavior and health needs.

Employers generally aren't allowed to know which individuals are flagged by data mining, but the wellness firms -- usually paid several dollars a month per employee -- provide aggregated data on the number of employees found to be at risk for a given condition.

To determine which employees might soon get pregnant, Castlight recently launched a new product that scans insurance claims to find women who have stopped filling birth-control prescriptions, as well as women who have made fertility-related searches on Castlight's health app.

That data is matched with the woman's age, and if applicable, the ages of her children to compute the likelihood of an impending pregnancy, says Jonathan Rende, Castlight's chief research and development officer. She would then start receiving emails or in-app messages with tips for choosing an obstetrician or other prenatal care. If the algorithm guessed wrong, she could opt out of receiving similar messages.

To steer more J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. employees to doctors in its network, insurer Cigna Corp. analyzed claims data to identify employees who lacked primary-care physicians. Those employees got personalized messages on Cigna's mobile app with recommendations for in-network doctors, says Michael Sturmer, a regional Cigna executive in the Northeast. Employees who had downloaded the Cigna app used in-network providers about 2% more than they did before the system was implemented in 2015.

Some people may feel uncomfortable with the idea that their personal data is being used to predict their future. Castlight carefully test-markets its messages to try to avoid appearing too intrusive, says Mr. Rende. "Every word matters," he says.

Maribeth Quinn says she was surprised to learn that her glucose levels put her at risk for diabetes, news she got in a message three years ago from HealthMine Inc., which says it analyzes health and claims data, but not other personal data. Ms. Quinn, a financial-aid executive at JFK Medical Center in Edison, N.J., has since lost 35 pounds, and is no longer deemed prediabetic. "It was in my face," she says. "That made me do something about it."

Mexico delivers unexpected rate rise with eye on inflation

Jude Webber in Mexico City 
892 words
17 February 2016
19:22
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

"http://search.ft.com/search?q=mexico&t=all&fa=people%2Corganisations%2Cregions%2Csections%2Ctopics%2Ccategory%2Cbrand&s=-lastPublishDateTime&f=regions%5B%22Mexico%22%5D%5B%22Mexico%22%5D&curations=ARTICLES%2CBLOGS%2CVIDEOS%2CPODCASTS&highlight=true"s central bank raised the key lending rate by half a point to 3.75 per cent at an extraordinary meeting on Wednesday in an attempt to nip in the bud the prospect of rising inflation.

The bank   which followed the US Federal Reserve in lifting rates in December when it lifted rates by a quart of a point, and had been expected to stay in step with the US   stressed its decision was not the start of a rate raising cycle.

It said it had been mindful of steps that the government and state oil company Pemex were taking to shore up the company s ailing finances. Pemex, Mexico s biggest company, which pays taxes that fund about a fifth of the national budget, lost nearly $10bn in the third quarter.

The peso has tested historic lows against the dollar persistently and lurched towards a psychological barrier of 20 pesos recently. On Wednesday it surged almost 5 per cent on the rate rise news to 18.2 to the dollar. It had shed almost 10 per cent so far this year against the dollar.

Mexican authorities until now have said the plunging peso would not spark an inflationary spiral, but the Bank of Mexico (Banxico) said in its statement that international market volatility had increased since December and the outlook for the Mexican economy had  continued to deteriorate .

The impact of that on the peso had  increased the probability of inflation expectations that are not in line with the consolidation of the permanent objective of 3 per cent , Banxico said.

 This does not kick off a hiking cycle but we will be following very closely all the inflation parameters,  Agustn Carstens, Banxico governor, told a news conference with Luis Videgaray, the finance minister.

The unexpected rate rise comes as Mexico is preparing to make new cuts in public spending for next year s budget and to throw a financial lifeline to Pemex, the state oil company, where stagnating oil production and a plunge in international prices has pushed the company into a financial crisis. The government has made clear that help for Pemex is conditional on Pemex helping itself by slashing costs and unproductive projects.

Mr Videgaray said federal spending would be cut by 132.3bn pesos, equivalent to 0.7 per cent of GDP   in line with previous belt-tightening last year. He stressed spending on security and social development would not be affected.

As part of the new austerity drive, Pemex would cut its budget by 100bn pesos, the minister added.

Alfredo Coutio, Latin America director for Moody s Analytics, said the actions went in the right direction but  could be taken as a sign of weakness, particularly because they are taken out of calendar .

He added:  The cost of delaying the adjustment could be higher since the measures are taken in a more deteriorated environment. 

The government has yet to announce what form its help to Pemex will take but Mr Videgaray said it would be up to the new Pemex chief executive, Jos Alberto Gonzlez Anaya, to announce details of the cuts next week. Pemex announces 2015 results on February 26.

 The priority should be to preserve projects that have an immediate impact on production and projects that do not have a positive impact on the net present value of the company should be cancelled,  Mr Videgaray said.

The government said there would be a weakening of growth as a result of the spending cuts and the Pemex rescue plans, but did not say by how much. The spending cuts will be spelt out in the coming weeks, Mr Videgaray said and should be  manageable 	. 	. 	. 	We are analysing options .

Banxico by law transfers excess profits from its operations to the government and the finance ministry says they are set this year to be significantly higher than last year, when they totalled 31.4bn pesos. Some in the market think they could be 10 times that level. Mr Videgaray said some of that cash, which is transferred to the Treasury in April, could be used to help out Pemex, but gave no more details.

Mr Carstens said that bank intervention to prop up the peso would now be made only on an exceptional basis. The bank has been auctioning dollars for months to try   in vain   to stem the peso s depreciation. He said it was hard to specify all exceptional circumstances but said they could include occasions when there were  large deviations in the exchange rate that cannot be explained by fundamentals and temporary factors and respond to a very low volatility in markets .

The peso has been hammered, in part, because it is used by emerging market traders as a proxy   it is the most liquid "http://on.ft.com/1RGebaw". Mr Carstens said he expected that pattern to continue. But he said the exchange rate had been taken to  levels which were not appropriate .

Mr Videgaray, who has long stressed that Mexico is being hurt by external shocks, said the dynamics of market intervention were being altered, but not the principle that  the exchange rate is set by the market .

Mexico has a 70bn credit line with the IMF but that would only be used as a  last resort , Mr Carstens said.

Business
Intersection Names New CEO Amid Push to Bring Free Wi-Fi to New York City; Al Kelly, former longtime American Express executive, becomes chief executive

By Nathalie Tadena 
801 words
17 February 2016
14:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Intersection, the technology and media company behind the"http://www.wsj.com/articles/new-york-city-to-replace-pay-phones-with-free-wi-fi-1451970003", has named former American Express President Al Kelly as its new chief executive.

Intersection was formed last year through the merger of Control Group, which created digital public interfaces, and Titan Outdoor, an out-of-home advertising firm. Intersection is part of the CityBridge consortium of companies working to replace thousands of pay phones in New York City with free Wi-Fi hot spots to create the world's largest and fastest municipal Wi-Fi network, a project known as "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-future-of-public-wi-fi-what-to-do-before-using-free-fast-hot-spots-1453232580".

Mr. Kelly, who spent 23 years at American Express in roles that included head of the global consumer group, brings strong experience managing public-private partnerships to his new role. He has previously served as the CEO of the 2014 NY/NJ Super Bowl Host Committee and as chairman of the 2015 Papal visit to New York City.

Helping cities and companies work together is a  kind of model that is going to become increasingly important as cities are looking for ways to diversify their revenue streams," said Mr. Kelly, who is 57 years old.  They're looking for ways to create better experiences for their citizens and they're looking for ways for their cities to stand out as unique and innovative."

Under the LinkNYC project, at least 7,500 Links units will be deployed across the city's five boroughs over the next eight years. In addition to free Wi-Fi, the units will also offer device charging, free phone calls to anywhere in the U.S. and Internet-enabled tablets. LinkNYC, which is still in the early days of its rollout, is expected to bring in at least $500 million in revenue for the city over the next 10 years. The project is supported by advertising, with revenue split between New York City and CityBridge.

 We fully expect that other cities around the world will find interest in this kind of capability," Mr. Kelly said.

Intersection is owned by a group of investors led by Sidewalk Labs, an urban innovation company owned by Google-parent Alphabet. Dan Doctoroff, former CEO of Bloomberg LP and former deputy mayor of New York City, serves as the CEO of Sidewalk Labs and chairman of Intersection.

Mr. Kelly was an attractive candidate for the CEO role because of his experience scaling businesses as well as his experience working with both public entities and brands, Mr. Doctoroff said.

 We have a really incredible opportunity to integrate new technologies into existing infrastructure in cities that can provide benefit to cities, in terms of additional revenue, and for citizens, new services," Mr. Doctoroff said.  There is an appetite for our product and we are going to have to work really hard to fill the demand. What we need to do is scale and do it in a way that prioritizes the most appealing opportunities, and Al really is uniquely qualified to do that."

As Intersection's CEO, Mr. Kelly said he would help the company continue to serve existing clients, push forward on initiatives like LinkNYC and diversify the company's client base around the country and ultimately outside the U.S. The company has media assets in 30 markets around the U.S. Past work includes the creation of  On-the-Go" information kiosks for the MTA New York City Transit and an"http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10000872396390444358404577605353787904554"for OTG Management.

 We are not just showing people ads but improving lives for the citizens of the cities and transit authorities we work with," Mr. Kelly said.

 Our hope is that through much more of a move from static to dynamic advertising and the use of display screens that are multipurpose beyond just advertising that we will certainly open ourselves to a new set of companies that want to advertise [with us]," he said.

Unlike most types of traditional media that have struggled amid consumers' shift to digital platforms, the out-of-home advertising market has steadily grown. The medium, which includes billboards, transit signs and other outdoor displays, has benefited from its ability to reach mass audiences who can't skip over ads and the conversion of some signs to digital displays.

At the same time, outdoor media companies have been ramping up their analytics and measurement capabilities and further integrating technology into the user experience. Magna Global projects global out-of-home ad spending to rise to $7.6 billion in 2016 from $7.3 billion a year earlier.

NATIONAL NEWS
BoE course emphasises experience over theories; Monetary policy

By Emily Cadman 
495 words
13 February 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Want to be a central banker? A bit less modelling and a few more war stories are on the agenda.

Paul Fisher - the man who as director for markets at the Bank of England turned the theory of quantitative easing into a practical asset-buying programme - is to design and teach a new graduate programme for the BoE.

With the global markets in turmoil and central banks around the world experimenting with negative interest rates, the course's emphasis will be on experiences from people in the macro-economic trenches.

One student on a discussion board run by Rethinking Economics, the group campaigning for changes in the way the "dismal science" is taught, said the BoE's move was "finally an acknowledgment" their arguments had merit.

The group has been pushing for the profession - the reputation of which was bruised by its failure to predict or fully explain the worst financial crash since the Great Depression - to focus more on the topical study of economics and less on mathematical models.

Compulsory modules in the postgraduate certification will include financial regulation in practice, systemic risks in financial systems and the implementation of monetary policy.

Also on the syllabus is the role of macroprudential policy, a relatively new and largely untested addition to the policymaking tool kit, which includes interventions such as the capping of high loan-to-value mortgages.

Mr Fisher, who joined the rate-setting Monetary Policy Committee in the aftermath of the financial crash, is to retire this year after 26 years at Threadneedle Street.

He will retain an advisory role after he steps down from the Prudential Regulation Authority, which will include designing the new central banking qualification, to be taught both by Bank insiders and academics from Warwick university.

Tony Yates, a former BoE staffer and now professor of economics at Birmingham University, said the new qualification could help fill a void.

"Before this course, there was a hole in the MSc market left by the insufficiently vocational economic and finance courses, which leaves lots of fascinating and really hard stuff - about monetary policy implementation, for instance, or the reality of a bank balance sheet, or a central bank balance sheet - to be learned on the job," he said.

Other optional modules in the postgraduate diploma include many of today's hot-button topics, including big data analytics, behavioural finance as well as unconventional monetary policy.

The BoE said in a statement the new qualifications - which include a diploma, MSc and MRes - will "equip the next generation of central bankers with the technical expertise and practical knowledge needed to continue to fulfil the Bank's mission".

Backed by funds from The Institute for New Economic Thinking, a research group bankrolled by billionaire George Soros, last year a selection of universities began road-testing a new "core" syllabus that focuses on real world questions - including what inflates a property bubble.

Nasdaq Inc. said it would buy news release distributor Marketwired for an undisclosed price.

Marketwired, which distributes news releases and economic data to investors and media companies, has been owned by OMERS Private Equity, the private equity arm of one of Canada's largest pension funds, since 2006.

Nasdaq, which is known primarily for its eponymous electronic stock exchange, has been expanding into more business services such as investor relations management. In October the exchange operator acquired SecondMarket Solutions Inc., a company that helped private companies facilitate trading of their shares.

In a release, Nasdaq said its clients will benefit from Marketwire's social media tools and analytics.  As our clients' needs continue to expand, we will be well-positioned to offer new tools, content, and services to meet their evolving IR and PR challenges and opportunities," said Adena Friedman, Nasdaq's chief operating officer, referring to investor relations and public relations.

The deal is expected to add to adjusted earnings within a year of closing, which is expected in the first quarter.

The Toronto, Canada-based Marketwired and other competing wires such as Berkshire Hathaway Inc.'s Business Wire were allegedly hacked and unreleased information was used facilitate illicit trades. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-charges-nine-in-big-insider-trading-scheme-1439295551".

News release wires have come under scrutiny for their relationships with high-frequency traders. In 2014 "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702303802104579449351569200252" to stop providing the releases directly to high-frequency traders.

Nasdaq shares rose 1.5% to $60.87 in morning trading.

Lending Club announces $150m share buyback

Ben McLannahan in New York 
636 words
11 February 2016
16:53
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Lending Club, the world s biggest marketplace lender, announced a share buyback programme and stressed its durability in a downturn on Thursday, as it attempted to turn round its free-falling stock price.

Since launching a decade ago the San Francisco-based platform has pumped out billions of dollars of loans, mostly by matching consumers keen to refinance expensive credit card debt with investors looking for high returns in a very low interest rate environment. In the fourth quarter last year Lending Club originated $2.6bn of loans, up more than 80 per cent from the same period a year earlier, earning fees of $134m, up 93 per cent.

But in recent months the company s stock price has collapsed amid fears that its machine will sputter in a downturn, once losses start to climb and investors turn away in search of better risk-adjusted yields. The shares dropped 56 per cent last year, ranking rock-bottom on the S&P financials index, and are down another 38 per cent this year.

On a call with analysts on Thursday, chief executive Renaud Laplanche tried to put such fears to rest, stressing that Lending Club had the flexibility to tweak its terms to take account of broad concerns over the US economy, and specific fears over defaults on the lower rungs of its 35-grade pricing system.

He also raised full-year revenue and profit targets and announced a share buyback of up to $150m, which is equivalent to about 6 per cent of the shares outstanding at current prices.

Net income during the fourth quarter came to $4.6m, compared to a net loss of $9m a year earlier.

Lending Club s rearguard action is an attempt to distance itself from other marketplace lenders which put their own balance sheets at risk, earning interest income and reserving for losses. According to Mr Laplanche, his company is better positioned because it serves as a simple matching engine, with notes issued to investors corresponding exactly to the loans it originates. If demand starts to flag on either side, he said, he can turn dials to ensure that returns to all kinds of investors remain attractive enough to ensure steady flows of fees for origination and servicing.

Lending Club has done so in recent weeks, putting up rates for some of its riskiest borrowers.

 We don t pretend to know more than anyone else about where we re going but in times of uncertainty it pays to be prudent,  said Mr Laplanche. The recent move to increase rates for certain classes of borrower was  pre-emptive , he said, designed to  provide additional loss coverage  to investors.

 It is a very dynamic process, and a benefit of our "http://ftalphaville.ft.com/2016/02/04/2152142/the-online-lending-lie/", to be able to discover pricing equilibrium in a very nimble and quick way,  he said.

The buyback could amount to about one-sixth of the company s cash and near-cash on hand. Carrie Dolan, chief financial officer, said that the company would be left with a  robust operating cushion  of cash for strategic investments and acquisitions.

Analysts warmed to the pledge to buy back shares, which prompted the stock to spike by as much as 13 per cent on Thursday morning before settling about 6 per cent higher on the day.

But some noted that Lending Club s continued commitment to strong growth   expecting its operating revenues to grow from $427m last year to $735m this year   could be hazardous.

 Sophisticated hedge funds have analysed the credit quality of Lending Club s issuance on a loan-by-loan basis and think [the company] is lowering the quality of their borrowers, which could lead to trouble,  said Justin Zhen, co-founder of Thinknum, a data analytics company.

FT REPORT - RECRUITMENT
Technology pioneer's success stems from excitement of science; At Work with the FT: Minerva Tantoco, New York City, chief technology officer

By Sarah Murray 
1,175 words
11 February 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys REC1
03
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Her mother demonstrated chemical reactions in the kitchen, writes Sarah Murray

Minerva Tantoco was "definitely a nerd", she says of her school days. "I had glasses. I enjoyed math and I didn't care who knew it. But I was extremely strong and athletic, so no one messed with me."

In this respect, little seems to have changed. With a cool, easy confidence and dry sense of humour, Ms Tantoco - an avid cyclist and dragon boat racer - comes across as someone who does things her own way.

This has served her well during her three decades in technology. She has held senior positions in advertising, tech and financial services companies. For her present role, she has moved out of the corporate sector and taken on the formidable challenge of working for New York City.

As its first-ever chief technology officer, her to-do list ranges from fostering technology entrepreneurship to extending home internet access to the 22 per cent of city residents who still lack it. She also promotes Stem (science, technology, engineering and maths) education among girls.

When I meet Ms Tantoco, she is just back from Nasdaq, the tech-focused stock exchange, where she joined a group of students and coding organisations for the day's opening bell ceremony. "It's all in a day's work," she says. "And every day is different from the next. That's what I tell kids - technology is so fun."

For Ms Tantoco, the "fun" started early. Raised in Queens by Filipino parents, she was soon drawn to science and technology. Her mother - who trained as chemical engineer and became a computer programmer in her 40s - would demonstrate chemical reactions in the kitchen.

"That seemed very exciting to me," Ms Tantoco says. "And on my mother's side, there were a number of very strong scientists and engineers. So it was considered normal in my family to be a scientist, engineer or physician."

From her father, an attorney, she learnt the art of persuasion. So when her parents resisted letting her study at the highly selective Bronx High School of Science (requiring a lengthy commute to what was then a dangerous neighbourhood) she argued her case and brought them round.

It was not the last time she would deploy her persuasive skills. In the early 1980s, she convinced her parents to let her interrupt her studies in artificial intelligence, cognitive psychology and neuroscience to join a Silicon Valley start-up called Manageware.

She met the founders, three Harvard Business School graduates, during a summer trip to California. Overhearing their conversation about designing a system to automate management consulting strategy, she was intrigued. "But none of them were computer people," she says. "So I walked over and said, 'You guys don't know what you're talking about.'"

Impressing upon them that they needed her expertise, she joined the start-up and convinced her parents this was a wise course of action. "I promised to come back and finish my degree in the spring," she says. "And we sold the company five years later in a successful exit."

In the late 1990s, as the internet transformed business after business, Ms Tantoco joined the advertising industry in the role of chief technology officer (CTO) just at the time she was expecting her daughter. "I was the first pregnant CTO," she says.

After developing mobile and wireless services at software specialists Palm in the early 2000s, she moved into financial services and started applying artificial intelligence to workflow tools. "We couldn't find a solution so we invented it," she says. Her team's work resulted in the successful application for four patents.

Some of her banking sector experience would prove helpful in government. As the director responsible for data architecture, she led the redesign and implementation of Bank of America Merrill Lynch's investment banking data warehouse, a project similar to New York City data analytics initiatives.

While at UBS in Hong Kong, in 2014 she received an email inviting her to apply for the New York CTO position: "The more I spoke to people, the more I realised this was a once in a lifetime opportunity."

After accepting the job, there was a slight hitch: "I'd already signed up for a 300-mile bike ride from New York to DC for climate change." This meant telling Bill de Blasio, the city's mayor, she could not start on the appointed date.

"He gave me this look," she recalls. Once again, Ms Tantoco's powers of persuasion would prevail - she arrived at City Hall only after completing the bike ride.

Career Clips

Did you ever consider another career?

I went into college thinking I'd become a doctor. But in one of my courses in psychology, I used this new thing called a mainframe computer. I was like, "Wow - now I know what I want to do." It was one of those things where your future finds you.

What mistakes have you made?

When I was in banking, I went out to our office in Beijing and told the president that we needed more women in technology. She took me to the tech floor said: "It's 60 per cent women." I should have done my homework.

How would you advise a young person entering your field?

If you want to be an innovator and inventor you have to have the vision that will allow you to press on, even when everyone else says you're crazy.

What was the biggest adjustment moving into government?

The public nature of the job - being a very private person, it was anathema.

On the desk

Minerva Tantoco's office is not your average chief technology officer's workspace. Around her is a cavernous chamber lined with white Corinthian columns and decorated with chandeliers and big gilt-framed portraits of former city mayors, New York state governors and other political and military figures.

The chamber once housed courts but Michael Bloomberg, the former mayor, turned it into something resembling a trading floor. Known as the Bullpen, he worked there surrounded by his staff.

A glance across Ms Tantoco's desk provides some clues about her life. A Bronx Science cap is a reminder of her school and an NYC Tech + Innovation notebook points to her role in promoting IT entrepreneurship. A screensaver image shows her with Bill de Blasio (at 6ft 5in, 1.96m, the mayor towers above her) and City Hall's dragon boat racing team.

Her favourite object is a red 3D printed toy (pictured), a gift from Michael Dubno. The Bronx Science alumnus and former IT executive made it in his basement lab, Ms Tantoco says.

"This makes me feel like I'm holding the future in my hand. It's the combination of the information age and industry and that's so fascinating to me."

Sarah Murray

Tech
Hortonworks Seeks Elusive Path to Profit Off Big Data ; Business software firm reports fourth-quarter gross profit of $21.7 million, $179.1 million net loss for year

By Elizabeth Dwoskin 
677 words
11 February 2016
00:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The market is growing for software that helps companies crunch immense amounts of data, but the path to profit is murky.

Hortonworks Inc., which reported its fourth-quarter earnings Wednesday, closed its first year as a public company by beating analysts' revenue expectations while burning through so much cash that it raised follow-on funding.

The company reported revenue of $37 million for the quarter, a 196% increase over the same quarter last year, and gross profit of $21.7 million for the quarter, compared with a loss of $46.2 million year-over-year.

Hortonworks, like many other companies that aim to profit from the rising tide of data flowing from connected devices, sensors and apps, is growing quickly as corporate customers try their hand at mining data to gain insights into their business. The company's customer count has more than doubled to 800 since it went public in December 2014, adding Mayo Clinic, Symantec Corp., Progressive Corp. and Noble Energy Inc. to the list. The variety reflects "http://www.wsj.com/articles/for-investors-hortonworks-is-a-big-data-bellweather-1446855724".

But that growth hasn't come cheaply. Hortonworks loses more money than it earns. For the full year, the company reported a net loss of $179.1 million on revenue of $121.9 million.

In January, the company issued 8.4 million additional shares in a follow-on offering to raise $100 million, pricing the offering at $9.50 a share, down from $16 on the day of its IPO. The company spent very nearly the same amount on operations in 2015.

The follow-on funding  in the current environment points to urgency," Oppenheimer analysts wrote in a report last month, when downgrading the company to perform from outperform. Many other analysts are bullish on the company.

 We believe our proposition is unique and adds tremendous value to our customer base," Hortonworks Chief Executive Rob Bearden said during a conference call.

The stock price plunged after the announcement earlier this month, capping a year in which Hortonworks took a beating in the public market. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hortonworks-prices-ipo-at-16-a-share-1418348055", its shares have plunged from $26.38 a 65% pop on the first day of trading to $7.91.

Some of the decline reflects wider trends as the stock market batters technology companies, hitting vendors of enterprise software particularly hard. Tableau Software Inc., a business software company with a mission to help customers  see and understand data" lost half its value last week; Splunk Inc., another big-data company, lost a quarter of its value. Hortonworks shares fell 17% during last week alone.

Some analysts think Hortonworks will emerge as a strong proposition.  When the fear and uncertainty dissipates," around the stock market,  I think you're going to be hard pressed to find a growth profile as attractive as this one," said John Rizzuto, managing director of software equity research at SunTrust Robinson Humphrey.

However, Hortonworks' status as a young company with limited cash reserves means it will have a harder time weathering the storms, Mr. Rizzuto acknowledged.

The nature of Hadoop makes Hortonworks' business particularly complex. Hadoop is distributed under an open source license, meaning the software is free for anyone to modify or use. The company sells support and services around the free software, including deploying consultants to work with customers, writing code and fixing bugs in Hadoop to suit their needs. This headcount-intensive business is similar to that of Red Hat Inc., the only other public company to commercialize open source software.

Although Hortonworks often compares itself to Red Hat, there is a crucial differences between Linux, a ubiquitous operating system whose code is part of the core infrastructure of the tech economy, and Hadoop, which is notoriously difficult to use and whose utility remains somewhat experimental.

Business
Dawn Lepore Discusses the Path From CIO to CEO; What the former Charles Schwab CIO and Drugstore.com CEO learned along the way

928 words
10 February 2016
03:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

As technology's role in business continues to grow, the chief information officer arguably has become one of the most important people in the C-suite.

But what does it take for a CIO to become chief executive?

Dawn Lepore knows. Before becoming CEO of Drugstore.com Inc., the online retailer she led from 2004 until its sale in 2011, Ms. Lepore was CIO at Charles Schwab Co., where she helped build the brokerage firm's successful e-commerce business.

Ms. Lepore sat down with Nikki Waller, who heads up management coverage at The Wall Street Journal, to discuss what it takes to migrate from a technology role to the corner office. Here are edited excerpts.

Journal Report

* Read more at "http://www.wsj.com/public/page/journal-report-leadership.html"

More in CIO Network

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-mistakes-companies-make-with-big-data-1455075886"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/gen-michael-hayden-gives-an-update-on-the-cyberwar-1455076153"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/andy-ozment-on-information-sharing-and-cybersecurity-1455082611"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/jeremy-bailenson-peers-into-the-future-of-virtual-reality-1455082679"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/andy-bryant-says-cios-need-better-communications-skills-1455083007"

The right balance

MS. WALLER: Dawn, you went from CIO to CEO in about five years. How did you do it?

MS. LEPORE: I think it was a little longer than that! You know, I was lucky enough to work for Schwab, which is a company where technology is very strategic. And I think that's one of the most important things as you're thinking about your career as a CIO: Make sure you are with a company where technology is the product, or technology is critical to the business.

I also was CIO at a time when the Internet came along. Schwab came out with Internet trading very early, which allowed us to get some visibility and allowed technology to be viewed even more strategically.

And then I was able to take on other responsibilities. So by the time I left Schwab and I was at Schwab 21 years I had a number of things, including our business for very, very active traders.

MS. WALLER: What is the most underestimated part of the shift from CIO to CEO?

MS. LEPORE: I think it depends on whether it's a public or private company.

I happened to go to Drugstore, which was public, so [that meant] dealing with investors, the earnings calls, the quarterly cadence, which is both good and bad.

On the one hand, you've got a report card every quarter.  This is what I said I would do." And  Did you do it or not?" So, in some ways it's a good structure and good discipline.

But it's also difficult. You've got all sorts of things going on in the company. Some of them are going better. Some are going worse. How do you communicate that?

You can't be overly optimistic. But if you're too pessimistic, then your stock goes down. That piece of it was difficult, especially the first year.

MS. WALLER: Is it easier for CIOs to go on to be CEOs of their own company, or do they need to leap to another kind of business?

MS. LEPORE: I think it's easier to go to another company, especially if you've grown up in a company. I also think it's easier to get a CEO job at a company that is very technology based. Drugstore, for example, was a retail company, but it was completely technology-enabled.

Emotional blackmail

MS. WALLER: What can CIOs do to be seen as business leaders and not just tech leaders within their companies?

MS. LEPORE: I was never a particularly technical CIO. I had some technical training, but I didn't have a computer science degree. I was actually a music major. So the business aspect of it was always more interesting to me. I was always drawn to that. So if that's the case, it's probably a little bit easier to be viewed as a businessperson.

One of the things that one of my bosses said along the way when I was CIO was,  You never emotionally blackmail me, and I really appreciate that." And I thought,  What does that mean?"

And he said,  You know, I've seen so many tech people who come and say,  Well, I can cut my budget, but I can't guarantee the system's going to stay up, you know.' "

So some of it is how you deal with senior management of the company. That's going to be part of how you're judged as a business leader.

JOHN BUSSEY: What common behaviors must a CIO stop doing to become CEO?

MS. LEPORE: One is that emotional blackmail that I was talking about before. The other is too much tech speak. I have seen, or been in situations, where the head of technology almost uses the complexity of technology as,  Well, if nobody else can understand it, then they must need me, right? And so I try to make it sound as complex as I possibly can, and I use lots of acronyms." That doesn't go over well.

The third thing is really making sure that you understand the business of your company, and that you can engage in very strategic discussions about the direction of your business and your competition.

If all you talk about is technology, that's going to limit you.

Challenger banks move to stand out from the crowd

Laura Noonan 
830 words
9 February 2016
05:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Here lies the big bank that once loaned billions to local businesses, but is now unwilling and unable to support the economy. Fondly remembered by its risk-adverse managers and overzealous regulators, its passing is deeply regretted by local business owners. Rest in peace.

The eulogy for banks  business lending has been on the chalkboard for years as massive losses, regulatory pressure and more nimble competitors threaten a perfect storm.

Surveys from the Bank of England chart an almost uniform contraction of lending to UK businesses from banks and building societies in recent years, despite initiatives such as Funding for Lending, which lets commercial banks borrow funds cheaply from the Bank of England so that they can pass it on in the form of cheap lending to companies. A similar downward trend in lending is evident across much of the developed world.

Meanwhile, investors globally have raised almost $80bn to invest in direct lending funds in the last two years, according to data from industry watchers Preqin. New peer-to-peer lenders such as Lending Club and Funding Circle are aggressively hunting for market share, and even insurers want a piece of the lending game. And policymakers including the European Central Bank are pushing for a more developed bond market to make it easier for companies to access non-bank finance.

Yet like other industry experts, including those that do not work for banks, Steve Dwyre, who heads Lloyds Bank lending to global corporates in industry, technology, media and telecoms, insists the narrative is not simply one of alternative lenders such as crowd funders triumphing over unpopular and ailing banks.

 There s probably too much focus on the crowd and peer-to-peer [sector] which is very small and may well be a flash in the pan,  says Conrad Ford, who runs "https://www.fundingoptions.com/", an online marketplace where small businesses can choose between different capital providers.

Mr Ford analysed a sample of 50 UK small business loans that were sourced through his website in the first nine months of this year with an average loan value of 123,000.

He found that just 18 per cent of loans were met by crowd funding, peer-to-peer lending and wealthy individuals.

Deposit-taking banks were a smaller percentage, at 14 per cent. Still, Mr Ford thinks banks  lending efforts should not be dismissed to the extent they have been.  You have two [UK] challenger banks which came about the same time as crowd funding, both of them individually have lent far more than the entire crowd funding industry,  he says.

The banks, Aldermore and Shawbrook, have collectively loaned more than 10bn, versus the 5.5bn that has been lent by the UK s peer-to-peer sector and crowdfunding combined, according to the "http://www.altfi.com/data/indices/UKvolume".

Bank lending is enjoying a helping hand from new forces in the market as well.

Credit Data Research is offering credit  passports  to small and medium-sized enterprises with detailed analysis of their finances that makes it easier for them to obtain loans from banks, which are wary of losing money, and to access market finance. The company started out in the Italian market 16 months ago. This year it will expand to the UK, France, Spain and Portugal, says founder Alessio Balduini. It has backing from Moody s Analytics, the analysis arm of the rating agency giant.

The biggest lenders in Mr Dwyre s space   accounting for 68 per cent of the 50 loans he analysed   are wholesale funding and debt funds, whose lending is typically short-term.

Among larger companies, funds and wholesale markets (serving banks and other financial institutions) are often the suppliers of choice for longer term debt. Doug Paolillo, an analyst at New York-based data firm Preqin, says direct lending funds typically target loans of five to 10 years  duration to companies with annual earnings of between $5m and $95m.

Mr Dwyre insists alternative lenders  success in this space is not a problem.  We don t want to have the longer term debt on our books because it costs us more,  he says.

 We don t compete on product, we compete on developing a relationship with a client,  says Alison Rose, head of commercial and private banking for Royal Bank of Scotland. When RBS  can t help  customers, it introduces them to other lenders, she adds. The British Business Bank, a government-owned service that helps small businesses to obtain funding, wants banks to be compelled to refer small businesses to other finance providers if banks reject their loan applications.

Lloyds is also looking to act as matchmaker between its clients and their ultimate non-bank lenders, by building out its capital markets business.  That s the big change that came out of this [last few years],  says Mr Dwyre.  We ve been forced to think about who is on the other side of this trade.

FT REPORT - TOMORROW'S GLOBAL BUSINESS: RAISING CAPITAL
Challenger banks move to stand out from the crowd; Lending

By Laura Noonan

Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Risk-averse incumbents prompt small businesses to turn to other providers, writes Laura Noonan

Here lies the big bank that once loaned billions to local businesses, but is now unwilling and unable to support the economy. Fondly remembered by its risk-adverse managers and overzealous regulators, its passing is deeply regretted by local business owners. Rest in peace.

The eulogy for banks' business lending has been on the chalkboard for years as massive losses, regulatory pressure and more nimble competitors threaten a perfect storm.

Surveys from the Bank of England chart an almost uniform contraction of lending to UK businesses from banks and building societies in recent years, despite initiatives such as Funding for Lending, which lets commercial banks borrow funds cheaply from the Bank of England so that they can pass it on in the form of cheap lending to companies. A similar downward trend in lending is evident across much of the developed world.

Meanwhile, investors globally have raised almost $80bn to invest in direct lending funds in the last two years, according to data from industry watchers Preqin. New peer-to-peer lenders such as Lending Club and Funding Circle are aggressively hunting for market share, and even insurers want a piece of the lending game. And policymakers including the European Central Bank are pushing for a more developed bond market to make it easier for companies to access non-bank finance.

Yet like other industry experts, including those that do not work for banks, Steve Dwyre, who heads Lloyds Bank lending to global corporates in industry, technology, media and telecoms, insists the narrative is not simply one of alternative lenders such as crowd funders triumphing over unpopular and ailing banks.

"There's probably too much focus on the crowd and peer-to-peer [sector] which is very small and may well be a flash in the pan," says Conrad Ford, who runs Funding Options , an online marketplace where small businesses can choose between different capital providers.

Mr Ford analysed a sample of 50 UK small business loans that were sourced through his website in the first nine months of this year with an average loan value of 123,000.

He found that just 18 per cent of loans were met by crowd funding, peer-to-peer lending and wealthy individuals.

Deposit-taking banks were a smaller percentage, at 14 per cent. Still, Mr Ford thinks banks' lending efforts should not be dismissed to the extent they have been. "You have two [UK] challenger banks which came about the same time as crowd funding, both of them individually have lent far more than the entire crowd funding industry," he says.

The banks, Aldermore and Shawbrook, have collectively loaned more than 10bn, versus the 5.5bn that has been lent by the UK's peer-to-peer sector and crowdfunding combined, according to the Liberum AltFi UK Volume index .

Bank lending is enjoying a helping hand from new forces in the market as well.

Credit Data Research is offering credit "passports" to small and medium-sized enterprises with detailed analysis of their finances that makes it easier for them to obtain loans from banks, which are wary of losing money, and to access market finance. The company started out in the Italian market 16 months ago. This year it will expand to the UK, France, Spain and Portugal, says founder Alessio Balduini. It has backing from Moody's Analytics, the analysis arm of the rating agency giant.

The biggest lenders in Mr Dwyre's space - accounting for 68 per cent of the 50 loans he analysed - are wholesale funding and debt funds, whose lending is typically short-term.

Among larger companies, funds and wholesale markets (serving banks and other financial institutions) are often the suppliers of choice for longer term debt. Doug Paolillo, an analyst at New York-based data firm Preqin, says direct lending funds typically target loans of five to 10 years' duration to companies with annual earnings of between $5m and $95m.

Mr Dwyre insists alternative lenders' success in this space is not a problem. "We don't want to have the longer term debt on our books because it costs us more," he says.

"We don't compete on product, we compete on developing a relationship with a client," says Alison Rose, head of commercial and private banking for Royal Bank of Scotland. When RBS "can't help" customers, it introduces them to other lenders, she adds. The Business Bank, a government-owned service that helps small businesses to obtain funding, wants banks to be compelled to refer small businesses to other finance providers if banks reject their loan applications.

Lloyds is also looking to act as matchmaker between its clients and their ultimate non-bank lenders, by building out its capital markets business. "That's the big change that came out of this [last few years]," says Mr Dwyre. "We've been forced to think about who is on the other side of this trade."
